RATING: 7
Rating based on 1 play.
Fun game, but not as fun as I hoped given the theme.  Really it is just a solid euro set-collecting optimisation game.  But a very clever one.

Unfortunately it has a couple of excellent newbie-traps in making sure you have the actions/resources left to buy a "big stadium" event in the last turn AND have a big stadium. I fell into that trap, which is pretty gutting.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Great theme, great components, fun, has randomness (yuck), but fortunately some of this can be mitigated, and definitely has some strategy.

Yes, it has some similarities to Princes of Florence, but I own and enjoy both.  I think this is definitely a game with a much wider audience.  There are only 5 rounds, but the game does take some time to play (more than the 60-90 minutes listed), as there are several phases each round: investment (limited action purchasing of items), auctioning, trading, putting on shows, and cleanup each round.

The way the game is scored is one of its best features: you only score for your "best" performance, which will probably be your last in the game, which results in revealing the winner only at the very end.

RATING: 5
This is one of the few games I actually won at the '07 Oasis of Fun. I also managed to win a copy on the prize table! So you can see why I'm off to a good start with this one. I think this is definitely the most gamer oriented game that DoW have put out. I also see the comparisons with Princes of Florence. However, I think the game stands up well by itself. It is a beautiful game, with a lot of fun planning and some tense moments. The "Intense Auctions" variant is a must, and the trading phase must be done quickly. With those two caveats in place, I would never turn down a game. One of the top 2007 games! UPDATE: I think that three player is the way to go here... the extra players don't add much except time. I think Prices of Florence should be played with four or five. Leave Colosseum for the three player games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A beautiful production, which feels like a re-theme of Kramer's older classic, Princes of Florence. Colosseum is simpler, with more interaction, and feels less of a heavyweight title. But it's a great game in its own right. Ultimately, the key mechanic is set-collecting. This is done through auction and trading phases, in order to score victory points. The key innovation here is the scoring system which makes perfect sense. Ultimately, regardless of how well a player has played in the early rounds, only the more effective player in the final round will win. This depends on everything they've put in place previously in the game, so doesn't feel unfair, but it can produce a surprise turn-around in fortunes. Excellent stuff.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This was a bit deeper than I thought it would be.  Days of Wonder have certainly done it again, though this game tends towards the medium level of euros rather than the light.  Yet, as with all DoW games, the presentation is such fun that it feels light, anyway.

The key seems to be to plan ahead.  You need to think 2 or 3 events ahead rather than just one.  You might not need Chariots now, but do your chit strengths mean that you are likely to later?  And, for instance, priests are needed for all of the 5 most prestigious events, so if you get one, it's probably not a good idea to trade it early.  You might want to keep it, but if you do trade it in, it'll be worth more later for sure.

The roll and move for the 6 bigwigs is a bit more thoughtful than just any old roll and move and you have to decide whether it's worth playing an Emperor medal to affect it.  Personally, I think buying an Emperor's Loge early on is pretty critical.

Although there are elements of PoF in the assembly of your events, Hollywood Blockbuster in the bidding, the game I really felt I was closest to whilst in play was Civilisation during the bidding phase..."I'll give you 3 salt for that gold - no, make that two gladiators for that chariot."   The trading is really very key in this fun game.  You can certainly lose the game right there.

So - critical trading, thoughtful roll and move, bidding, strategy (in what you buy and what events you pre-plan for), theme, luck, an interesting set of multi-ability jokers...all good stuff.  What more could you want from a mediumish Euro?

RATING: 6
A semi-interesting game with auctions, trading and resource control, plus a smidgen of dice-rolling.

The limited availability of events, and the limited number of actions, means there's more player interaction (some of it subtle) beyond the auctions and negotiations (trading).

The theme and connection thereto pushes this from a 5 to a 6 for me.

A well-enough done game, but I'd much rather play Princes of Florence.  The two are [i]very[/i] similar in many ways, including the auctions, the limited actions and the completion of events/works (of limited availability) to earn income.  Theme is much more apparent and better implemented in Colosseum and there's more player interaction, but Princes feels smoother with more interesting choices that enable more strategy and may play faster since there are no negotiations to resolve.

I would play Colosseum occasionally, but I would be willing to play Princes much more often.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This game's problems start with the victory condition which makes only what the players score on the last turn matter.  The previous four turns are just preparation for that final scoring.  A strategy that I and others have won with is to deliberately score the least in the first four turns to gain my choice of tile from the "leader" at the end of the turn.  That advantage (three tiles in my last game) may seem small, but in a game where so much else is balanced, it can be critical.  An effective strategy this may be, but fun it's not. Layer on top of that a build phase that is formulaic because there are just five turns and four, maybe five things that must be built to stay in contention.  Plus an auction system that barely functions and trading that is plain dull.  And none of this is helped by money being both plentiful and of no value in the final scoring.  Finish with a slice of luck in the form of the wandering Caesar and his consuls; just pray that more than one is in range of your colosseum come the final turn.  And despite the low number of turns it takes a long time to play. 
Rarely have so many elements that don't quite work been cobbled together in one game.  The remarkable thing is that it doesn't fall apart entirely.

RATING: 7
A great game for people looking for something beyond Catan.  Plays up 5, plays best with 5, but is still enjoyable with 3 or 4.  Games typically last around 90 minutes (although the first game may take considerably longer).  The components included are all fantastic and abundant, almost overflowing the tight-fitting box.  The rules might be a bit of a read, but the game makes sense and is easy to get into due to the cyclical nature of each round (you invest in your arena, you bid for assett tokens, trade, run your event, then prepare for the next round and repeat).  The only thing that new players may have difficulty with is  planning ahead for future events.  Nevertheless a perfect "next-step" game and a lot of fun all around.

The luck of dice rolls for moving the emperor, consuls, and senators is also a factor but not significant.  The emperor's favors are a factor.  It may be possible to run an excellent 3 show and beat a 4 show, but it is unlikely.  Many people might be expecting (or wanting) a deeper game, but I think this fits in very well with the depth and style of most Days of Wonder games.  It is a descent game to add to a library but not a must have.

Ami nekem meglepő volt, hogy nem nagyon adta vissza az aréna hangulatát, pedig a díszletek adva voltak hozzá. A szenátorok körben mászkálása számomra kicsit erőltetett, és nem mellesleg elég nagy szerencsefaktort is visz a játékba. A lapkák meg még ennél is többet. Ezzel együtt azt mondom, elég sok féle nyerési út kínálkozik, a mi játékunk tanulsága számomra a következő: a győzelemhez nem muszáj megépíteni a legnagyobb arénák egyikét (20-as ünnep lett végül a nyertes 91 ponttal), és a 2 dobókockás épület nem ér meg egy akciót szerintem. A játék végén a pénzből elég nagy bőség volt, ez meglepett. Ja, amit majdnem kifelejtettem: a pontozás nagy ötlet szerintem, a seelandban is visszaköszön hasonló. Hogy sportos hasonlattal éljek: nem az összpontszám számít, mint pl. a legtöbb labdajátékban, hanem a legjobb eredmény, mint az atlétikai számok nagy részénél (ugrások, dobások). Kézenfekvő, de valamiért nem divat a játékoknál ez a fajta értékelés.
Összességében szép játék, és a helyén kezelve szerintem jónak is mondható. Máskor is játszanék vele szívesen.

RATING: 7
[i]Posted: 2/10/09, Rated: 7[/i]
{3P: ???, 4P: 7, 5P: ???}

Ratings are subjective, pure and simple.  Thus, let me tell you about my preconceptions regarding [i]Colosseum[/i]: I suspected that it was a bad game.

Days of Wonder offered a copy [i]for free[/i] in December of '08 if you spent $50 or more on their site.  One copy of [i][thing=21348]Ticket to Ride: Märklin Edition[/thing][/i] later, and I owned both titles.  What kind of game do you give away for free?  "One that isn't selling well on its own," was my reply and also the reason I suspected the game was bad.

Was I ever wrong.

[i]Colosseum[/i] is a good game.  It might even be great, but we'll get to that in a moment.  First, let's talk about the absolutely ridiculous production values.  Days of Wonder is known for their lavish offerings, but they've really outdone themselves this time.  The sculpted noble meeples are exquisite.  The artwork is detailed, pleasing, and is highly saturated in color.  The theme has been consistently applied in every aspect of the game.  I love how [i]Colosseum[/i] looks on the table.

It must be the game play which comes up short, then, right?  Wrong again.  If anything, [i]Colosseum[/i] is too deep for its own good.  There's an investment phase, with seemingly equal choices which in reality are not; an auction phase, which is going to paralyze new players who don't yet have a handle on valuing tiles; and then a trading phase which is also difficult for new players to quantify.

A good starting place is to compare the relative benefit of the items in question to the receiving players.  At this point, the game feels calculation-heavy, like the worst parts of [i][thing=2651]Power Grid[/thing][/i].  If you believe that [i][thing=2651]Power Grid[/thing][/i] has no "worst parts," then go out and secure a copy of [i]Colosseum[/i] immediately.  All of these auctions and trades should be pursued with a long-term strategy in mind: producing a show whose components you are trying to acquire throughout the game.  Try to select your end-goal at the very beginning, and hope the show you want to produce is still available when you're ready to purchase it.  If you're counting, there are at least three mechanisms in play which are going to require a deeper understanding before the game starts to "click."

Scores for the round are tallied using that same calculation, everyone suffers some attrition, and then there is a charity step where the poorest gets to steal from the richest.  Wash, rinse, and repeat for five rounds.  The trick is not to set too high of a score early on: the charity step serves as an inducement to choose your shot wisely -- a fourth mechanic which requires several plays to appreciate.

[i]Colosseum[/i] looks great, hides surprising depth, and is an exciting title for veteran players.  Why doesn't it earn a higher score then?  Perhaps those preconceptions are still seeping through.  Or maybe it's because competition at the higher ranking levels is very fierce.  One thing is for sure: I'm looking forward to playing it again.  Only time will tell if it has true greatness, or if it is merely a crumbling façade.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5

RATING: 8.2
Will never be one of the great games but is certainly one of those hovering around the margins of greatness. I return to this one every once in a while and it always repays me.

The jockeying for position is reminiscent of Power Grid - nobody wants to come first, you want to come a close second or a close last and the investment decision at the start of each round is a tough one (5 rounds, one buy per round, many things to buy). Highly enjoyable, though ultimately marked down a little because it is smashing with 5, but only good with 4 and not so good with 3.

RATING: 9
Colosseum comes with the cutest little meeples I've ever seen. It's also one of Days of Wonder's best games. The rulebook is a bit longer than your average DoW offering, but it's a very easy game to learn and to teach. 
You'll want to do so much in this game, but there's just no time do accomplish it all, which means that there are some pretty brutal decisions to be made. In spite of this, the game moves briskly and does a good job of keeping every player involved at almost all times.
The unique theme is pulled off beautifully.

RATING: 6
There only 5 rounds so you need to study the tile & show combinations that work well together or else you will end up going in multiple directions and not score enough points.  There are too many types of asset tiles and not enough of each (alternatively, you could say there aren't enough wild tiles), especially considering the rapid loss of tiles via retirement.  Annoying that there is not a superstar for each asset type, especially because almost everything has the same rarity (10-12 tiles in the bag).  With 3p you don't see enough tiles (partially due to the fact that there are so many stinking kinds of asset tiles) & there is little trading.  With any number of players, you really need to pay attention to who is getting a slam dunk and progressing from one show to the next perfectly... then the losers must gang up on the winner(s) and take the most important tiles during the "steal" phase.  

Completing shows in step-wise fashion is critical because each completed show provides a +5 bonus to your next show.  I would like to try adding a 6th round for the 3-player game.  The noble movement trend is 2 laps around the board for the 5p game and only 1 lap for the 3p game (probably due to fewer dice rolls in the 3p).

UPDATE.  Haven't played in a long time but my residual gut feelings are as follows... it's an okay auction game but nothing stellar, someone pays a bunch and gets what they want, everyone else just kind of does the best they can with sloppy seconds.  It plays better than that sounds and there were some ways to mitigate the luck and limited choices, but (in my opinion) it is maybe just a little above average as far as auction games go.

RATING: 8
Set collection, auction drafting, and infrastructure building set in ancient Rome. Players gather "assets" (lions, horses, gladiators, comedians, musicians, scenery, etc. etc) in order to stage productions in their individual colisseums. Productions and venues start off small but opportunities to enlarge your venue (small, medium and large) and mount ever more spectacular productions are part of the game. Of course, larger productions require more room and more assets and cost more to "get the rights." 

The game lasts five turns - the end of each turn is where you stage your production and collect the prestige (VP) it merits along with an equivalent amount of money. At the start of each turn you have one opportunity to make a capital investment which could be enlarging the venue, buying the rights to another production, adding a loge, or the provision of season ticket holders which add to the prestige of the production each turn. As there are normally only five such opportunities, a lot of thought needs to go into which investment you make and in what order you make them.

The acquisition of assets is done via auction in lots of three. Each turn, a player will acquire a set of three assets (you start the game with six random assets). The lots are "open" so you can see what you are bidding on. After the auctions are concluded, there is a Settlers style trading phase where players can trade assets between them. Before staging (and scoring) your production, you roll one (or two) dice to move pawns around the board from venue to venue. The pawns represent influential persons you wish to attract to your venue (Emperor, Tribunes and Senators). The presence of these personages add more to your prestige.

The game is beautifully produced - I'm constantly impressed by the quality board game production is exhibiting these days! Gameplay is brisk and a five player game only takes about two hours to play. Although the initial draw of assets and productions is random but very important, the fun factor is decent - I would play it again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
It's 'ok' - lovely bits as you'd expect from Days of Wonder, but lovely bits a great game doesn't make ...

It's a mish-mash of stuff you've seen before, and all about investing and timing along with auctions and random chit pull. And an element of sitting in 2nd place and sprinting past at the end like powergrid.

I'd play it again, as there is an element of long-term planning and there are various routes to victory making it interesting. Luck can overwhelm though, chit replacement means you can press your luck and wait to see if something better appears and then get it cheap, pull it off and you do well, if the right chits don't appear ...

So, all seems good, why the '5'? I don't much like the dudes moving around the track, their bonus is really rather important, and if you get them early then it's an income boost, but later is more vital as really you're going to score your best show in the last round. Which makes the frst rounds all rather pointless, you're just building your tile set and income and stadium for the last big bash.

Ends up being a game I'd play if someone else was keen, but not likely to suggest it and no desire to own a copy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I love it. It's probably my favorite game centered around an auction. There is so much fun to have in the game's flexibility, and the uniqueness and utter playfulness of it's design. I never see players experience AP playing this euro because the game feels so intuitive. There isn't the counting and agony over decisions that Power Grid has, and while it's fairly tight the game allows you to keep fitting more and more into your turns, which is so rewarding. Got to be one of the funnest euros, though if you don't like randomness or trading that might be a deal breaker. Try it with the fan-made 6 player expansion, as it may even be better with 6 than with 4 or 5. A great game experience, just remember to have fun and describe the shows you're putting on (especially if you were missing pieces and you have to try and describe the entertainment value of watching a single gladiator and a plant pot). Not as honed or precise as Power Grid, but definitely funnier, a bit more raucus and, dare I say it, perhaps more fun.

EDIT: One thing I should note, we always play with "free" trading, where everyone just trades with whoever. I also recommend that, with fewer than five players, you play with the intense auction variant as it adds needed tension to the bids. Indeed, this is a game where you may need to try a few rule variants to see what works best for your group. Some may be irritated by that, but I find it a very flexible game.

RATING: 8.5
Great game. Light, yet fraught with tough decisions. Cute, but very competitive. This is a family game with teeth. 

You might think this is just another simple set-collection game. You would be wrong. The auctions make it critical that you make more money every turn, especially if you want to stay competitive, but you need at least two turns to put on a larger event: one to expand your stadium and another to buy the bigger show. This means you are always planning in advance, sometimes three or four turns in advance, gathering performers you will want at endgame. But since everyone else is doing the same, you really need to keep an eye on what they are doing lest two of you go for the same show. The end result is a pretty thought-provoking, medium-weight Euro that feels really light and simple. A perfect combination.

RATING: 5
Sigh. This is reasonably fun, but the hopelessly broken Emperor's Loge is a bit of a red flag. That this is so mis-costed it isn't funny - it could be at least triple the cost and probably still be underpriced. Maybe quadruple. The best answer might be to give everyone one for free at game start, but then one hates to muck with the total number of build phases received, it might screw things up. Regardless, anyone who doesn't buy one on turn one will not win. I absolutely guarentee it.

Other than that, this is a kinda fun game - I like the bidding followed by a dealing phase, sort of like Die Haendler. But things somehow don't feel right here. Lots rarely get bid up (even with the "advanced" rules) because the floor price is rather high, and then there isn't a whole lot of dealing, because of the way the items are used (there are a number that are useful for almost anything, then others that are used in tandem, and with only 3 up for bid in any given lot its unusual to bid for a lot with much in it that you don't want).

The theme is nice, but I think this is an example of a game whose theme is sapped by sub-standard gameplay. If the bidding or trading phases were more lively, with more competition and more opportunities for deal-making, and if people were forced to deal more to get what they wanted, I think the theme would work a lot better. But the balances all seem off, and the so the tension isn't what it should be, so the game lurches where it should glide.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
[05/04/2007] This appears to be one of those games (Zug um Zug would be another good example) in which you're executing a once formulated game plan as the game encourages players to specialize and carve out their own niche in the world of Roman showbiz. 

The winner is bound to be the one who formulated the best game plan and managed to implement without it being derailed. This may make the way future turns and the endgame shape up quite predictable and therefore prove somewhat anti-climactic.

AUCTION PHASE:

Most auctions tend to be pretty dull affairs due to the degree of specialisation going on here. The special chits do occasionally jazz up an auction, but the auctions don't add anywhere as much excitement and fun to the game as in the thematically quite similar Traumfabrik. 

Really, specialisation leading to relatively lacklustre auctions is exactly the same problem which dogs Phalanx Games' Emira (though to a yet greater extent). A game which is equally puts great emphasis on formulating a game plan and pulling it through.

TRADE PHASE:

Am I the only one finding straightforward trading getting rather stale and potentially tedious? Yet leaving this personal gripe aside, I feel the trading here lacks also tension. 

The degree of specialisation simply makes certain attractions worthless to each player. Makes trading a little too straightforward and lacking any downside. What to trade away and what to acquire is usually quite obvious. Merely the exact price becomes the issue.

Yet what really irks me about the trading phase is that money doesn't appear scarce enough in this game. This isn't offset by money being of consequence in scoring. Money thus may almost become worthless in trades. A player with good cashflow of his own simply does not need any further money.

This issue of mine is of course tied to my experience of most auctions not being too contested. Were the auctions pricier, money would be scarcer and thus also once again of greater value as a trade commodity. But as said, specialisation discourages for the most part auctions spiralling high without any special tiles being involved.

ROLL & MOVE:

This element being added to the game seems like a rather desperate attempt to jazz up the game given that its auction phase does not generate the fun and excitement Ra, Traumfabrik or Modern Art generate.
Questionable design, IMO, as the roll & move stuff really doesn't add much depth or fun to the game. Not to mention it being a pretty unthematic manner in which to portray the political élite's favour.

CONCLUSION:

The auction, trading and roll & move mechanics all aren't particularly successful in this game. Perhaps that's why they were all thrown in as a (not particularly convincing) compensatory measure.

So where's the heart of the game? It's in the formulation of a master gameplan. That involves a detailed study of the whole spectacles play aid. A little "spreadsheet analysis" going on here. That may be intellectually challenging, but is it FUN?!?

Don't get me wrong. This is a solid, albeit rather workmanlike, game. It works. But, rightly or wrongly, I expect more from one of the most respected designers out there with such winners as Torres, Maharajah or El Grande under his belt. 

This game gives me in many respects an Emira déjà-vu. Fortunately, it works a lot better than that somewhat troubled game. I don't dislike Colosseum, but I can't say I'm overly impressed either. I miss the elusive spark which really makes a game FUN!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Colosseum is a very interesting game which I really enjoyed (the game was very close and all four of us could have won so it was extra exciting). We played with the official intense auction variant which, by reading the rules, seems much more interesting. The trades afterwards were perhaps slightly boring as you would often end up with lots of stuff and didn’t really want to trade a lot, especially at the end. However, I don’t see this as a negative as the trading in the first place is not what makes Colosseum such a fine game.

There are several reasons why I liked Colosseum. I liked how you needed to collect different tiles to run your shows (and that you had to discard a tile afterwards). There are several shows you can invest in during the game, but if you want to have a shot of winning you need to expand your personal colosseum so you can host bigger races. This also helps when you move the pawns on the main board which you use in order to maximize points by making them visit your colosseum, or when you move the pawns to get medals which can be used for various things during the game or at the game end. I liked how you roll dice and move the different valued pawns and how the main board was shared with each player’s own colosseum.
 
But what I really loved about Colosseum and what makes it such a unique game is the victory condition! You win the game by having the highest score in any of the 5 rounds; your points don’t cumulate in each round which is ingenious and a very refreshing mechanic. Also, because of this you don’t want to be in front in the beginning but rather slightly behind so you can steal a tile from the leader. Of course you can’t be too far behind as you won’t get enough money to use on the development of your arena and outbidding the others for the good tiles, so it’s something you need to balance. 

All in all Colosseum was a big hit with me and I would love to play it again and perhaps even purchase it. However, it’s OOP so I might accept that I’m only going to play it once a year at Djengis Con like I did this time. We were four players and I feel it was a good amount of people. I think 3 and 5 would be fine too as the auctions aren’t really that interesting compared to other auction games which really need 5 players. 
Oh, an update: I got the opportunity to buy this game, took it, and have played it a couple of times more. It's still innovative and great! Highly recommended.

RATING: 5

I don't think this is a bad game by a far shot, but it never really occupied my thoughts with ways to better my play after finishing. Just very bland.

RATING: 5
Colosseum = Wolfgang Kramer to revising elements of several old game systems (yet again) and putting together a game that is close to working, but not quite there.

Colosseum is a game of investments and timing. The core idea of the game is somewhat similar to a combination of Princes of Florence and Leonardo da Vinci with a smattering of Power Grid. The PoF and Leonardo comments are because it is a game of long term planning, where you acquire items (PoF) to meet certain menus that are presented to you (Leonardo). Like Power Grid, staying in the back of the pack can be a very good thing.

The basic structure of the game is simple: buy a thing (i.e.: a fresh menu, an upgrade for your colosseum). Then win some elements of an exciting show in an auction. Then possibly swap some items around. Then move the various personages around the board with dice. Finally, put on a show and get some cash. The best singular show presented in the game is the winner.

So this game wants to appeal to my long term planner side. It is possible to be planning for the last turn on the first one with this, as the different menus are all available for purchase early, and buying the various show elements in lots of 3 means that knowing what you want immediately, and what you'll want down the road is important.

Yes, there is certainly luck here though. The way the bonus point hand-out pieces move is dice-driven, and though you can affect those odds, they still can burn you, badly. The way the lots are replenished can often be an important random aspect as well, particularly if you want a certain tile or tiles, but they just will not show their faces.

All of this said, I initially found this an enjoyable enough medium-heavy planning game. It's not as serious as most of the games that it borrows from, but it seems to carry its theme decently well (even though it is abstracted) and I think it offered enough twists to give it some legs.

On the downside, after a couple of plays, I am starting to wonder about the effect that the pawns have on the game. The scoring bonuses they offer are extremely important, and the last turn of the game, where the big show is held, seems to hinge heavily on where they end up. This sours me on the game experience as a whole because so much seems to ride on that last turn. If you can position the pawns where you need them for the final stanza you'll almost undoubtedly win this, and too often that ability to control the pawns is out of your hands.

One final caveat, this might be a family game for those of us who have families who play a lot of Euro games, but this is not something I would buy cold for non-gamers. The system has a lot of nuances at work, so even though there are random elements that give the less experienced a chance to get some decent deals, the overall learning curve here is fairly high.

Overall: almost a solid design. Too much rides on the final turn pawn positions for my tastes though, and I am not particularly interested in playing this again, though I would at least be willing to with some light arm-twisting.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Create lavish Roman Colosseum shows by acquiring assets via auction and negotiation with other players.  Then try to bring important people to view your production for extra points.  Some fun, but play can run a bit long, especially when using house rules which it really needs for the negotiation part.  Negotiating in turn order is completely broken, as it leaves the last player totally out in the cold, because all other deals will have been made by then.  Instead, the negotiation phase is best with a free-for-all style.  Some variants allow for multiple auctions to be one by a single player, which extends play time dramatically and increases the chance of having a runaway leader.

Roman Colosseum shows - Auction, Negotiation, Dice Rolling, Set Collection, Trading
(3-5, [b]best w/ 5[/b])
_Weight 2.8
♥ 90 min
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Another Days of Wonder gem.  Great production value... looks great on the table.  Rather than dice rolling and gladatorial combat, as you might expect, this is an auction game with some interesting decisions to be made.  One of the most intriguing features is, the scoring is not cumulative.  Whoever has the single best round over the course of the whole game wins.  So, you're never really out of it for sure, which gives everyone a chance right down to the wire.  The only drawback is that the rules are a bit more involved than other DoW games like Ticket to Ride, but once learned, they are intuitive and lots of fun to play.

RATING: 6
The auctions aren't very interesting, and considering this is a game largely built on an auction dynamic, this is disappointing. The production values of this game are off-the-hook, but what underlies the bling is a fairly non-interactive experience that largely revolves around picking a third-stage event program, rather dully acquiring a tile set to fit it, occasionally moving around a few pawns to your area to augment your spectators, and trying to hit one big score. I initially thought a dynamic where your final score is just whichever is your round's best (in 5 rounds) would be a novel dynamic, but in reality, there isn't enough meaning to each of the individual rounds. So, it's an OK game. Maybe it'll grow on me with another play or two, but someone else will have to nominate this one hit the table again....

RATING: 7.7
A very pleasent game.

It´s very well balanced and conceived, it fits very well into the theme and forces the player to have some skill management over the (short) available money, using it wheter to upgrade its structures, bid on tiles or buying licences for shows.
Also very nice ideias are the ways of trying to capture senators, consuls or the emperor to attend one´s show (more points/money awarded) and the scoring system i believe is unique: The final score is given only by the greatest score (value of the show) achieved in any of the five rounds of the game (usually the last one, but not necessarily so).

RATING: 5.5
Colosseum is an interesting mix of resource management, auctions, and Diplomacy style negotiations.  It is a very solid game, but it has a few aspects that don't sit well with me.  

The main thing I dislike that there much of my time is spent sifting through a multitude of options in order to make one decision.  I feel like I need a secretary to keep track of everything when I play this game.  If only my secretary could lay out the viable options, then I could look the few remaining options make a decision about what to do.  Unfortunately, I'm the person who has to look through the chart of possible shows, then look through all of the show tiles to see the score of missing some of the components, etc.  This process grinds the game to a halt for the last 2 rounds, even with players who are not prone to AP.

RATING: 5
A beautiful production and a great unique theme (producing shows, that is... ancient Rome is not unique).  The trouble with this game is that it can cause intense analysis paralysis.  The game is wisely designed such that one cannot produce a series of shows all using the same resources, so you must adapt your resources between each production.  But it is easy to get trapped into trying to find that perfect combination and I see other players staring at the list of shows trying to figure out a perfect path through the game.

This should play in 90 minutes, but can go 60-90 minutes longer.  When that happens, it really drags.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Players are Roman producers, putting on a nice, expensive production down at the old amphitheater.  Players take turns to play through phases - expand, purchase, trade commodities in order to increase the lavishness of their productions (e.g. "Bacchus and the Nymphs").  Productions take place at the end of the turn, earning cash and a peg on the current victory point scoreboard.  The winner of the game is the player who earned the highest VP score at any point during the game (theoretically you can win on turn 1, I guess).  Large, colourful, lavish, and in this age where we've seen it all before, not a bad game at all.  Perhaps a bit prone to over-analysis in your first game or two, but it settles down and speeds up nicely after that.  A nice surprise. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Not sure I would enjoy the trading or some of the random elements thrown in here, not to mention the forced-catch up mechanism of last place player taking a resource from first place.  Seems like a family-friendisized Princes of Florence.

UPDATE: I bought it and played it a few times with my family, after trading Princes of Florence for being too dry.  This game certainly has more flavor, but is overall an even more milquetoast gaming experience.  The auction, ostensibly the central mechanism of the game, is frequently uncontested.  There is also potential for AP as people study the program card.  

That all being said, this is a very beautifully produced, pleasant family game, and I'll happily play it.  The catch-up mechanism actually works pretty well in the context of the game.  The limited actions possible between auctions keeps everything nicely under control.  However, it does make the gamer in me crave Medici for a truly great auction, and good ol Catan is a better trading game.  I could also recommend Vega Showdown as an auction game of about this weight that is overall more interesting.

RATING: 9.5
Wow!

Knowing how difficult it is to find this game I'm surprised I got to play this at all. It's got a lot of forward planning and can get a bit mathsy, especially on the later rounds, but it is a fantastic experience.
Each part of a round offers a diverse range of good options limited by your money and, especially in the auction phase, other players actions. Knowing when to upgrade, what tiles to buy or which you can trade for, what to negotiate with, what shows to put on and if you have the necessary conponents, all of it never wearing out it's welcome make this game absolutely amazing and deserving of a Days of Wonder deluxe edition.
It must also be said that the physical nature of building up your Colosseum with tiled components that make your arena come to life is one of the most satisfying parts of the game and serve to remind you that it isn't JUST an arena but YOUR arena. At the end you feel a sense of satisfaction in that.

Of all the games I've played this has been my favourite so far.

RATING: 8.5
I love the theme on this and the game play is very engaging and yet easy to pick up. 5 turns may have been a little long for a single game however 4 turns would have felt very short.

The game is really nice and the scoring only your highest show mechanic is nice but it does mean you can essentially clear up on the final round and never actually play the rest of the rounds.

If only a few players are moving the roman figures around it gets a bit skewed and therefore more difficult to catch up as you have to dedicate more resources to moving the guys further and less to the show you are putting on.

The tokens are really nicely designed as the colouring on them is very distinct and so you can tell from across the table what a player has a a glance. This makes trade discussions easier as there is no real hidden information.

RATING: 5
Rating after a single 3 player game. The material is gorgeous, with so much cardboard you feel you've bought a whole tree. The game, however, is bland. The similarities with Princes of Florence are numerous. The '''action phase''' is there, but it doesn't seem to offer the array of strategies available in PoF. Moreover, you are not really constrained to "5 actions during the game" since you can get medals and exchange them agains more actions. This really lowers the tension. The '''auction phase''' is similar, but there are so many lots that there is none that you really want. Consequently, bids stay very low and this feels more like a second "action phase". (This is exacerbated by the fact that selected lots are randomly replaced. Moreover, you can always trade for the tile you really need.) The '''construction phase''' is there also, but since points are not cumulative you don't really need to optimize each of your moves. In fact, your often better off finishing last with just enough money to keep going and an extra tile you steal from the leader.To top it off, this game is '''dominated through and through with luck'''. Roll dice, get guys in your arena: earn points. Roll dice, get guys on a starting spot, earn points or (better) extra actions. Draw tiles, get a joker, earn loads of points.I have to try it with more than 3 players, but with this number its no better than a 5/10.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Fun
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Colosseum is a beautifully produced game, and I am a Kramer fan anyway, so this is a winner, right? Well. The underlying features are all fine and dandy but there a few notable detractors. 

Obviously there is quite a bit of luck in the drawing of chits from the bag but the more problematic part is the die-controlled movement of the Caesar, senators, and consuls. They can cause huge swings, and whether you can get them to visit your arena or not is just too random. Another sore point is the completely open trading which can easily unbalance the game and offers great kingmaking potential. Somewhat stricter trading rules would have benefitted the game a lot, especially since money is otherwise quite useless.

On the plus side, since only your best round is important for the final scoring pretty much everyone is in the running until the final round although those who have won previous rounds may have acquired considerable bonuses on the way.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 2
While this may seem like a harsh rating, it's honest. It would be extremely difficult to convince me to play this game again.

Colosseum feels like a cross between Settlers and Power Grid, but generally fails to have the redeeming qualities of either.

There's way too many variables that are luck dependent. The figures that revolve around the track can make a huge difference in the game, and you have rather little control over them. The auction mechanic severely screws over whoever goes first. The fact that the last round is the only one that matters, and the fact that there's a mechanic that helps the player in last place means it's a perfectly viable strategy to do not compete until the end of the game. Power Grid may penalize the leader, but at least in that game you have to keep up with the joneses.

All in all, I thought Colosseum was severely flawed.

RATING: 7
Being a Kramer game and having a bit of a lineage from Princes of Florence, I was primed to like Colosseum. In the end, I think it was a fine game, one I'd like to play again but probably don't need to own. We played a 4-player game, which I think is the minimum; 5 players might be the best. One of the notable points for me was the uneven tenor of the game play. One moment, we were making jokes and laughing and the next, we were quietly studying charts and tokens to figure out what we needed for future rounds. This wasn't bad - just odd. I don't think I've ever quite played a game that went from fun to serious to fun quite so rapidly. I'd like to give Colosseum an 8 but there are a couple things holding me back.

PROS
-We played the Deluxe TMG version: metal coins were a nice touch as were the other components. I know people slag on the art but I like it. Also, the board is much simpler looking than the overwrought original.
-The trading phase was a highlight: bullying and cajoling opponents into making a deal was quite enjoyable. We played the open negotiation variant.
-Putting on the performance and counting up the bonus, while a little mathy, gave a satisfying sense of accomplishment.

CONS
-The auction was a total dud. There were only two auctions where people ended up paying more than the obligatory 8. 
-Maybe this is really only a 5-player game? Certainly no fewer than 4. Having 5 players would make the auctions a bit more interesting but that still might not solve it.
-Turn order might be a sticking point, especially if you're in a position where you don't see the Emperor for the whole game.
-The back of the player aid is hard to read and lacking critical info. I would prefer the old Days of Wonder version with the excel table. It also needs to have the lower values for each performance if you're missing tiles.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I prefer it rather than Princes of Florence. More interactive among players and IMO a game with more life. Good components by the way

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Colosseum is not going to be an evergreen in our rotation, but I did get several enjoyable sessions out of it before moving on.  It's a melting pot of mechanisms that plays something like a lighter, looser Princes of Florence mashed up with Traumfabrik.

Like:
1. The pointed structure of the game, with everything aimed at setting up one glorious final show.
2. The jockeying surrounding the tile that the last place player steals from the leader.
3. There are several viable paths to victory.
4. The wonderful resin pawns, and the overall look of the game.

Don't like:
1. The movement of the pawns is way, way, way too influential on the outcome.  This is the big reason I don't continue to suggest this game.
2. The auction is too loose.  Most lots go for the minimum, and you may or may not luck into what you need.
3. The "intense auctions" aren't a solution, because they wreck the game's central tension, scarcity of tiles.
4. The trading phase can be cumbersome if players aren't able to quickly assess whether there's a viable deal out there (a la Settler of Catan).
5. The game could stand to be a half hour shorter.

The pace of the game feels a little bit like Funkenschlag/Power Grid, where players have to consider the tradeoffs between running out ahead and hanging back.  Frontrunners won't have to worry about cash, and will earn extra laurels (podia and/or season tickets) for their final show.  Trailers will have an easier time getting the tiles they need for their final show, but might need to seek cash in the trading phase.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

A bit fiddly but I normally don't mind that. Beautiful components and the game play is solid for me. Will gladly play this any time.

RATING: 7
Colosseum certainly fits well in the Days of Wonder line.  It is not a “heavy-weight”game, but it is still filled with important decisions and strategies.  There is a considerable amount of planning, trading and strategy to employ in order to perform well and present high-valued productions.  There is a progression aspect present, as players must build on previous turns’ actions and productions in order to prepare for their ultimate gala show.  

I am thoroughly enjoying Colosseum, and each of my games has been tense, and filled with important choices and strategic options.  Each game feels different from the previous ones, and the problems presented seem unique.  Colosseum is a fun and solid game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
The game is basically a series of auctions followed by negotiations, but there is some fun and nice tension in trying to fulfill your programs. However, as each player can only win one of the auctioned sets per round, sometimes luck of the draw plays a big role when the market is refilled. A worse problem, though, is the pawns that move via die rolls around a track and may grant some important bonus points or quite useful medals, if they stop inside a player's arena or on marked spaces, respectively. Also, given that only the best round of each player actually scores, the position of the pawns in the final round is too important, as it may be very easy for a player to gain bonuses, and impossible for another. Last but not least, if two players are preparing for the same program, of course one of them will be seriously harmed when the other grabs it. Despite its flaws, this is still a pretty decent game, but the random elements bother me a little for the time and effort it takes to play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
You bid on the tiles needed to perform your shows from different lots showing.
If you have the most* of that type of tile in possession, you get a bonus. Usually. (some types do not have a bonus.)
You are free to negotiate with other players for tile/etc trading. Apparently the game we played was incredibly friendly compared to how it normally is haha, but I could imagine it getting quite cutthroat.

Anyway the goal each round is to put on the best show possible a la "contract completion". You get a score based on the value of the show/contract + special people visiting your colosseum + any bonuses you have. 
The quotations is around completion because you can be missing a few things needed to perform the show, but you earn less points the more stuff is missing. Also you can re-perform a show previously performed, though it seems to be more advantageous to generally perform a new show each round because there is an accrued benefit to completing new shows.

Whoever wins is the one that performed the SINGLE best show! So score at the end of each round is not a cumulative thing, but something that just tracks what your score was for best performed show up to that point. Interesting as you can't tell for sure who is going to win until the last round is done.

RATING: 7
Colosseum features a box-full of good quality components and a theme that, while rich, comes straight from the Mel Brookes school of history. I await the 2nd edition rethemed so that players are pizza parlour proprietors trying to dazzle their diners and visiting VIPs (local mafia boss, 2 film stars, and - why not? - 3 senators) with their cooks' current concoctions. The structure and mechanics of the game bear a debt to Kramer's earlier Princes of Florence, but the result is more like a souped-up Pontiac than a Ferrari FXX. Surely both an auction and subsequent trading for the 'Event asset tokens' is one phase too many. I also have concerns about the role of money; it features prominently and yet seems to have relatively little effect on the overall game. Perhaps the game authors intended money to change hands during the trading phase, but that has barely happened each time I've played the game. Other minor issues that can, at times, marr the game include the various random elements (particularly the use of dice) and the potential for king-making. Colosseum is certainly interesting enough for at least a few outings, but is unsatisfying as a game and so probably lacks long-term play value.

RATING: 4
The was a real disappointment for me.  I found it long and some of the longest segments were just uniteresting.  The trading was unecessary with the "intense auction" variant, but still time consuming with so many chips to consider.  It has the feel of Princes of Florence, in that you are trying to build on your pre-existing infrastructure to rise to ever greater heights.  But in PoF, the points are cumulative. RATING: 6
Auctions:trading/negotiating:set collection:roll & move

initial rating one play 5 players

I liked the overall feel of the game, a little like Princes but it felt even more like Traumfabrik in the auctioning and how the shows worked while the trading was a little reminiscent of Chinatown. Comparisons aside I do think the game stands well on its own and has a unique enough feel to it with some added elements. 

Turn order seemed a little too important and while it rotated, you can still be significantly burned by it if your coveted work gets snapped up before your turn comes around, same goes for the trading if you stick to the turn order method. This turn order method of trading/negotiating while fairly controlled bogs down and lengthens the game significantly as everyone waits for the current person to finish bartering. A possible alternative to this is to go all out all at once Chinatown style, probably more fun and faster but also much more chaotic, will have to try this and post results.

Forward planning is very much awarded and a gamelong plan is not only possible but necessary for the win ala POF.  While I don't usually like non-cumulative scoring (ie Tempus/Bison) I think it works well here with only 5 rounds and everything you do still having an impact on your endgame if you planned well. The Kramer variant allowing you to participate in more than one auction while lengthening the game a little adds to the game and the strategy I think as well so worth playing IMO. The chart for the events seems a little too busy with all those goods on there and it can be hard to pick one but this would get easier I'm sure with more play so just a minor nitpick there.  
  
The game actual still progresses along nicely aside from the points above until the end comes, I had issues there. There is the potential for kingmaking due to all the open information (could be tweaked by keeping the works hidden?) and while I didn't mind the dice/luck during the game to try and hit the bonuses it can decide a winner at the very end of an otherwise strategic game (could be removed for last round?), fine for a one hour game usually but for 2 to 2.5 hours it ends up being a little anti-climatic. Seems to have pretty decent replay and talk about a TON of bits included in the game. 

In the end I think it has a little too much luck/randomness for a 'gamers game' and is a little too gamey for family folk, this leaves it coming in as a long (maybe too long for a middleweight) somewhat fun middleweight which isn't totally bad but maybe not as appealing as it could have been had it focused on a clearer path. Could be 7.5 with tweaks and 6 or much lower without and might be better and of course shorter with less players (4 best?)

For a strategic negotiation game with no luck see Mr. Kramer's 'Die Handler'.

RATING: 8
This game is pretty good and I played this one before Princes of Florence. I enjoy both of these. The auction phase can create a bit of player interaction as you're trying to complete your shows and snag bonuses from other players. The biggest problem with this game is that a few poor dice rolls early can really hurt. Usually you can get at least get one emperor's medal a turn as long as you have an emperor's loge.


In this type of games its always important to try to predict what route your opponents are interested in going. This makes it easier during the auction phase to try to pick up valuable asset tokens that others will want/need which can be a handy strategy if you get outbid on a lot you want or your asset tokens simply aren't available for you.

The last game I played I learned to adjust my overall plan when the plan started to go sour. I originally tried to go Glads, Horses, Chariots and ultimately moved to Glads and Boats since noone seemed interested in it. I was also able to luckily trade off my extra horses and chariots since those became two valuable items. That game turned out very well and I ended up with a very high score.

RATING: 6.5
There's so much to like about this game that I wish I could give it a higher rating. The theme is wonderful and well-integrated into the gameplay and it's simply fun to play. The auction, however, usually lacks tension, and in none of my 3 plays has trading been a significant part of the game. Even with 5 players it seems difficult to find someone who has mutual trading interests, despite the fact that everyone ususally has several tiles that are worthless to them. A friend remarked that he felt the game had too many currencies. The fact that players may hold between them 9 or 10 tiles that they can't use, but still can't arrange worthwhile trades, suggests he may be on to something there. A shame, because so much of the game is nicely designed and scratches many of my most insistent gaming itches. I'd gladly play again, but I'm not sure I'd ever buy it

RATING: 6.5
Good game.  There is a lot of interaction with the other players, mostly in the trading phase.  You must always keep and eye on what the other players are drafting for, but if they are drafting for an event they have not purchased yet this can be tricky.  Fun game, even though it took us 3 1/2 hrs to play our first game.  Mainly because some of the rules leave room for speculation on how they work - for example in the rules you are asked to put the event tiles in numerical order and you can purchase events but you have to purchase a larger event than the one you have just performed.  Our question was do we have to buy them in order starting with the top event, later it became obvious that you can buy any event from the stack as long as it was a higher value of the one you just completed.  I believe our next game will be much quicker, but this game can take a long time because of the trading phase.  We played with the full compliment of players which I believe was the major contributor to the length.  I would like to play this game with 3 or 4, 5 players just makes for too long of a game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
A nicely produced game with too many actions that don't mesh well together. 

There were several things that bothered me about the game. The first thing is that there are five rounds in the game and in order to put on one of the larger shows you need to upgrade you arena twice and be able then buy the large show you want. That requires three of your five available buy actions during the game, which means you only have two other buys to make that are not predetermined. Now I am assuming in this case you don't have an extra build action to use. Which leads me to the next issue.

The emperor medallions are very powerful but are primarily gotten through fortunate dice rolling. I've played in games where I've seen players buy a loge first turn and only be able to get one medallion while others in the same game managed to get six medallions. Skillful play? Nope, timely dice rolls. The medallions can give you extra VP, additional buy actions, additional movement, or extra cash. The extra buy actions and VP I've seen to be huge advantages. In the last game we played one guy who managed seven medallions won with an extra buy and 15 additional points to his final show. Now this would be okay to me in a 30-45 minute game but this game plays in the 120 minute range and in that time frame I want less luck to play a part in the final outcome.

The issue I had with the auction phase was that no new tiles came out for auction as long as the active player has not won an auction. Where this is important is that in the five round game you really can't screw around since you'll need certain tiles to put on a decent show. So you're not going to try and jack up a bid on someone if you can't improve your own position with those same tiles. In a way it reminded me a bit of the Power Grid auction which I also have a problem with. It's that the last player can just fall into a great thing and walk away with it at cost which in Colosseum is $8. We had it happen in our last game when I was last player and the new tiles that came up include a pair of jokers. Getting two tiles like that go a long way and I was the only player who could bid on them.  

I could probably go on but those were the big things for me. It's not a game I'll search out to play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I had no intention of learning this game since I was around for the collective BGG sigh of disappointment when it was released. But sometimes it's impolite to turn down a game, and besides, I like the designer, the components are attractive, and I wanted to see just how accurate the "watered down Princes of Florence" claims proved to be.

Initially I nurtured some hope that the game would succeed as a more casual crowd-pleaser as opposed to an intense strategic experience. It proceeded more or less inoffensively, with no clear red flags but no "wow" moments either. And it kept going, for three full hours. Somewhere past the half-way point it hit me that, after the requisite 10 minutes of chart-pondering, the choice of overall strategy could be made fairly early, making the rest of the game an exercise in eeking out little bonuses for yourself and perhaps tripping up other players. Sadly, the mechanics mustered but a frail limp to aid me in that goal. Auction after auction went for minimum bid, sometimes with players lucking into extremely valuable bonus tiles after the other players were 'out.' Trading phases involved a lot of peering and double-checking and half-hearted offers of unwanted junk. Everyone knew what everyone else needed, and everyone seemed able to attain most of it via uncontested auction. Money in the game is plentiful. The only time you might actually examine the budget is if you luck into a tile that gives you an extra buy. If this were to happen to you on turn three, you probably have the game locked up.

I found myself wanting to like Colosseum for the presentation alone, and I'm d*mned if the range of shows you can put on isn't extremely cool, but this game just doesn't seem to work. At all. I'd happily accept an understudy to Princes of Florence, but this one isn't worthy.

RATING: 6
Seems quite similar to Princes of Florence.  At heart it is a very simple game with an overwhelming number of pieces.  The theme is completely laughable and unconvincing, but it is adequate. There's no way I would play if I knew it was going to take so long.  But if I wanted to play a game like this, I'd turn to Princes of Florence, every time.  PoF does everything this does in a quicker, more competitive and more satisfying way.

RATING: 6.5
A bidding / managing resources game with the usual DOW eye-candy and whole lot of cardboard. Its a little more rule-heavy than other DOW games, but still fairly accessible and fun to play.

There was a little bit of luck with the rolling, but overall the programs are designed such that there isn't much competition between players for resources. With a total of 12 different resources, I never found myself in any direct conflict with another player. Even though there was trading, the game was pretty solitaire and has been likened to PoF by other players.

What did impressed me was actually that you could pursue different strategies in the game and end up with very close scores. In the end, I'll probably own this because of the cool components more than the game itself.

RATING: 7
This game is reminiscent of Princes of Florence.  I'm still on the fence about this game.  Both plays (with mostly beginners) took around 2.5 hours which I feel is too long for this game.  Also, there are a couple of mechanisms that doesn't feel right to me:

1.  New tiles are not draw unless the auction initiator wins the auction.  If you are the first player, and what you need is not available, you are pretty much out of luck.  Also, in some instances, you can have many tiles drawn which only one or two players can bid on.  They might luck into a very good draw.  Such swings of luck is very discouraging in a longish game.

2.  It just seems like there is way too much to plan for in the game.  You pretty much want to plan for you biggest show early in the game.  But that is very difficult and induces AP.

[b]Purchase/Selling History[/b]:

[b]Rating: 5[/b]
[i]Design rating[/i]: [b]2.5[/b] 
[i]Component rating[/i]: [b].5[/b]
[i]Willingness to play rating[/i]: [b]1[/b]
[i]Fun rating[/i]: [b]1[/b]

[b]Players considerations[/b]: 

[b]My thoughts[/b]:
Always wanted to play this game because I heard it was long and complicated. My kind of game. Actually I find it simple and maybe long, but not too long with 4, and not so much complex as it just takes a bit to realize your first time playing that what you are doing is going to work out in some way. That's kind of what happened with me, I ended up finding a way to make the pieces I had work and ended up winning--sort of lucky imo. I like the variety of events possible, I bet some people could take advantage of this game and just go for a particular one every time instead of being creative with what they have. The figures moving around the track is not a very strong mechanic, as the emperor did not make it around to more than 2 player's arenas, so that is a definite advantage to the players immediately and subsequently left of the emperor.

In Colosseum each player is a Roman impresario - producing great spectacles in his or her arena in the hopes of attracting the most spectators. Players earn wealth and glory for each event run, using it to create ever more ambitious events. They will need to improve their arena, find the best performers, lure the Emperor and his nobles, and manage assets for long-term success to be granted the title of Grand Impresario, with tales of your extraordinary spectacles acclaimed throughout the empire.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Ok, summary opinion after just 1 play.  Could be so much better with less randomness.  

Your starting tiles, whether you get a +4 bonus for a 3 set, how they match your starting shows, what you roll on the dice(s) and how many medals that nets you all have a significant impact on how you will fare in a given game.

The mechanics are fine but skill & strategy is only part of it and there's actually the reverse of rubber banding.  The 1st player to get the +3 token will get 15g out of it over the game and a better shot at other +3 tokens which in turn give you more money and better shot at more tokens and final win.

The actual rubber banding (last stealing from 1st), although sorely needed, feels tacked on and is very disrupting to the planning of the 1st player.

If everyone play optimally, I feel that the only thing really preventing me to calling out a winner before 1st turn is the dice rolls.  Another way you could act on the play is to deny trades and/or pity trade but that would mean counting everything to know who the next round leader is and is hard to do accurately without slowing the game.

I'd play again for sure, but I have doubts in spite of the game's high ranking.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
solgt 13-01-15 til Legius Germangames, da det ikke var kommet på bordet (oop); 
købt Essen (2010)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
It draws comparisons to Traumfabrik, Showmanager and Princes of Florence but is very different on it's own.  There's lots of planning for which shows to create and deliberating of which tiles to gain.  There's a fair amount of luck in setup/start.  I wish the auctions or lot refreshments weren't so limiting on the "start player".  Acquiring medals is mostly a random roll of dice (and there is importance towards getting them).  Also, the high randomness of how the pawns (Emperor, Consuls & Senators) move definitely creates a significant "random impact" on the endgame results, especially for the very last show performed.  I think this has the potential of being a much better strategy game if the randomness is mitigated more and if the auctions were less limiting with more player control.  The artwork and production quality is fantastic (as you would expect from a Days of Wonder game) and the theme and bits are very pretty.  I am interested in playing the designer's variant(s) to see if that helps improve my taste for the game.

RATING: 4

Otherwise, its a mishmash of mechanics in which you seek to put together the best show possible to draw the most spectators. It's relatively easy to instruct, but may appear daunting at first glimpse with the number of pieces in the game. RATING: 6

The rest was really window dressing to me, so that i could trade again the next turn. Sure, I was trying to put on the biggest show, but what i will always remember about this game is how I ended up in a tie with another player at the end of the game after I got her to trade me her Emperor's favor, and all of her money just for two tiles. The theme is kind of pasted on, and honestly this is a big complaint of mine about the game. Putting on shows in Roman Colesseums should be exciting affairs, not min maxing your way to VPs with little tiles.

RATING: 8
Be an impressario in ancient rome. Great resource-matching game: Gather as many of the actors/requisites for the show you want to perform, and awe the spectators with your grand displays. Enlargen your theatre in order to attract influential officials as patrons, for further prestige.

Another game where it is important to stay off 1st place until the last turn or two, as the last place gets to steal a ressource from you after the shows have been performed. Interestingly, it is only your single best show that counts, making it possible to win the game with an earlier performance..

I somehow haven't mastered the art of collecting diverse enough ressources, which normally leaves me with a one- or two-trick pony show for last (but still winning!)

RATING: 7
Good auction game. Played only one 5-player game. The game was tense and exciting. I think the trading phase can be quite long especially if player argue to get an asset when the other don't want to give it. Should be played with stricker rules to keep the game from dragging.

What is particularly interesting is the winning condition. Even if you lose badly the whole game, if you prepare for the last big show you ca win. It keeps everyone on their toes up to the last turn. 

I would like to try the "advance auction rule". 

Update: Played with 3 and the advance auction rule. The auction didn't seem to be too different. With 3 the game is quicker but less tense. It is easier to get what you want and there is less trading. Medal and having the possibility to make a second investment are especially important since it enables you to make one of the big show.

RATING: 6
I see the parallels that people draw to Princes, but those ties are tenuous at best. I would actually liken the gathering of sets of tiles in this game to that of Ra, yet you cash them in to make a "work" like PoF. The individual tiles hold no value themselves though.

There is a good deal of luck in the tiles that come out and when, not to mention the fact that you have the die rolls to move the pieces around the track to gain bonuses and medals.

This game is WAY over produced.

There are more ways to gain points than in almost any other game I've seen.

This will either be a 9 with further plays, or drop to a 7.

The game does a really god job of balancing itself, and even one false move can be recovered from.

I think that like most Wolfgang games that this one will pick up steam with age, and as people play it more they will appreciate it more.

Update: Dropped to a 7 then a 6. The movement of the pawns around the track is awful. 2 players can bounce them between on another, and he who starts with Caesar can win most of the time. There's also little variety in the shows, and even less control in them than at first blush. 15 plays proves this is really a 6. Play Princes of Florence or Leonardo instead.

RATING: N/A
Game seems heavier than Pompeii and definitely more than Thebes.

Excellent comments:http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/583075/have-to-decide-to-open-or-return-this-game

Apparently the game is far simpler than Power Grid and Agricola; while scratching the same itch as PG BUT looking much nicer. More accessible to newbs and BGF but does have the "downside" of being too long for newbs at 1.5 hours (See article on game length: http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/569232/average-time-for-a-game-of-colosseum)

LOTS of pieces BUT apparently the game mechanic is VERY intuitive and all the pieces have a purpose, so this does NOT make it overwhelming for newbs. 

Amazon has 9/10 people rating it a five, so it looks like it'll very much appeal to non mainstream gamers.

May have a limited predictable strategy (where you "figure things out") after a few games (and therefore you'll be just going through the motions).

May not be a great gateway game due to: 
-- the layers of play
-- weight = 2.8  
-- stated 90 min game time (can possibly run longer)

Apparently the player aids assist tremendously in teaching newbies the game. And, the gorgeous layout makes the game quite pleasurable to play.

CON: Minimum 3 players

NB: I suspect that there are many more games to play out before this one will become a staple in the game library

Consider the following comments: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/583075/have-to-decide-to-open-or-return-this-game

RATING: 6
Game Designer: Wolfgang Kramer (El Grande).  I was very happy to finally get a chance to try this game, I had wanted to try it for years given the theme, the designer, and the fact that it is an auction game.  Beautiful components, and interesting game mechanics (e.g., the scoring method).  My main concerns are two: 1) I saw a runaway leader/snowball problem in the first play and problems with catching up (two of the players were ready to quit already halfway through); and 2) If one wants to win there is a lot of thinking/planning/counting that has to occur.  The runaway leader problem is one of my pet peeves.  It is a medium/heavy to heavy eurogame with a strong analysis/counting component and little to no luck or deception/bluffing.  I call these games the "chess" type games.  It is also fairly long even with experienced players, and I could see it getting even longer if people start to analyze the options/counting/scores.  Competitive and complex.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
One of my favorite games.  I like to use this game to introduce non-board gamers to what modern board games are really like - as a 'bridge' game. Many people have said that Settlers of Catan was their bridge game (and it in a way was mine too), but I personally don't love Settlers and think Colosseum is a better bridge game.  Why?  Because as I like to say "this game is certainly to be like NO other board game you've ever played."  It's that different, but very fun.  There's a bit of a learning curve at first, but there is enough repetition in the game itself (you go through 5 actions 5 different times; so by stage 3 or 4 players have the hang of it).
The game itself has it's flaws - experienced players have an edge, the bonuses each round skew things too much - esp on the final round, but all in all it's a very fun game that gets people interested in board game geekery!

RATING: 9
Rating after 4 plays. Beautiful components. After reading the rules I was reminded of Princes of Forence, but the gameplay feels very different - While PoF feels like exact science at times, Colosseum is a lot more free-wheeling: more random things and the trading phase makes the auctions somewhat less important.
This is very much my kind of game: lots of things to do, forward planning is important but there is enough room to make some adjustments to the plan.
Tough game with 5 players (because there are less resources in your opening 'hand' and because competition for tiles and events is tougher), very pleasant middle-weight game with 3 or 4.

RATING: N/A
In Colosseum each player is a Roman impresario - producing great spectacles in his or her arena in the hopes of attracting the most spectators. Players earn wealth and glory for each event run, using it to create ever more ambitious events. They will need to improve their arena, find the best performers, lure the Emperor and his nobles, and manage assets for long-term success to be granted the title of Grand Impresario.

Beautiful material. Original implementation of a very appealing theme. 

In this game you bid for actors and material for your show. In this way you try to organize an event that attracts as much spectators as possible.
If you plan well your best event will take place during the last (5th) round of the game.

RATING: 7
Big, glossy production and an original and fun game to boot. After one play, I feel that the similarities to Princes of Florence are overexagerrated - a lot of the less enthusiastic responses to the game might stem from playing it like Princes instead of, well, Colosseum.
My enthusiasm was dampened by the open trading, which is bad enough in games with more hidden information. Here, it both drags the game down and offers ripe opportunities for kingmaking and the bad kind of bashing the leader. Even so, I'd gladly play again, but a more stratified trade mechanic would elevate Colosseum to an 8 or even a 9, which is where I'd consider buying it.
Played twice.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5

Overall I am a big fan of this game and it's been in my top 10 for some time now.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Great game, simpler than Tribune, but still get the same sense of theme.  A different flavour of VP track, with non-cumulative scoring, simply marking the best event staged at the end of each round.  No real benefit in trying to be the leader - lose an extra tile in exchange for an ongoing 3VP bonus.  The game rewards building towards a final push in the 5th and final turn.

Need to be careful to hold on to the bonus tiles until they can be used most efficiently (especially trading in to spike the value of the last event).

Really enjoyed this game - there is a lot to like about it.  My first play was with 5 players and ran for roughly 2.5 hours (rather than 90 mins).  The trading was reminiscent of settlers of catan. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.75
A good game, but you have to be wary of players in last place near the end of the game. They potentially can play "kingmaker" by giving away their assets to other players and upsetting your hard earned awards to deny the leader bonus points.

RATING: 8
Wonderfully produced game from DoW that allows players to take on the role of Event Managers in the mighty Roman Colosseum.

The game is all about putting on events to impress the Emperor and draw the crowd. The players must acquire the planning rights to host an event and then acquire the many components that make the event what it is. 

The game combines auction elements with a series of other euro mechanics like the positioning of the key figures to create a fun but somewhat taxing experience.

I really enjoy this one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
As a Roman with an arena, build up your arena to attract more spectators and run shows. On your turn, choose one way to expand your arena (build new show, build season's tickets, etc) or buy a new show, bid for resources for your show, roll dice to attract Roman high council members, and produce your show. The trick in this game is that the highest value show overall wins the game, there is no cumulative counting of scores. Game lasts 5 rounds, and the person who produces the highest value show in each round is penalized by having the person in last place take a resource. This is a nice component game, very fun to play and where you have to build up for your final show.
