=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Studying the back of the player aids for the programs that'll give you the most overlap with your amassed tiles makes perfect sense, but boy, is it not fun.  There's also a significant runaway leader element, and a great deal of harm can be dealt to your initial momentum if there's no tile overlap on your first two programs.  Worst of all is the "no refills" rule which takes effect when an auctioneer doesn't win the tiles he initially bid on.  This creates incentive for everyone to just let the auctioneer win the bid, so there will be a bigger menu of stuff to bid on as soon as he/she is out of the bidding process;  a very boring way to conduct auctions.

There's fun to be had, but it's fragile.  There's randomness that creates tension and a need to stay alert, and there's randomness that simply creates large gaps between the players' scores.  Colosseum, I'm sorry to say, has a great deal of the second type and very little of the first.

RATING: 5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
No one would guess at first glance the amount of strategic thought required to do well in Colosseum: A player should be thinking about the final show from game start as there is only 5 investment phases, 2 of which are needed to expand your stadium, and 1 to buy that show's ticket. Not to mention assessing the available resources and plotting a course to have the necessary components to put on a good performance. 

Which leads me to Colosseum's 2nd unexpected gameplay feature: Ruthless competition for components in the resource market and their subsequent trading. Because everyone can see what the other players have, and need, even novice players will be very cagey in their trades, never wanting to give another player an advantage.

Do not play with 5 players. The game length gets unwieldy, but most importantly, the 5th player is at a disadvantage due to where his/her colosseum is located: Everyone else has access to a Rest if they expand their stadium in a certain direction, but the 5th player doesn't.

The advent of a long-awaited reprint filled me with worry that my beloved 1st Edition would be outshone by a new version requiring me to repurchase the game. Not so! The Tasty Minstrel reissue is inferior in every single component. What a relief for my wallet!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Set collection game with a tiny bit of trading mixed in.  The theme is one of putting on great shows for the spectators to draw a bigger crowd every round.  In the end whomever puts on the show that brought the most spectators will win the game, and to do this you must cleverly place the influential politicians roaming the board, pick up special abilities that grant you more spectators, and even draw on super stars that will attract even more spectators for your event.  

Event cards are used to give you an idea of what tiles you will need to perform a certain show.  You will trade to try and acquire the tiles needed, but you also will have much bonuses that will increase the spectators coming to your show.

This is an easy game with some decisions that is great fun to break out for non-gamers.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
Fun concept, but not a terribly compelling game for me. There's a disconcerting amount of unmitigatable luck involved here, between the initial hand of tiles you're dealt, the initial tickets you're dealt, the specific combinations of tiles that come up, and the rolls of dice to move the Roman senators -- enough luck to sway the outcome of the game. By the end, everyone in the game is going for a different ticket, so the auction component ends up being highly un-interactive, with no one wanting anything anyone else wants, making it an auction of no competing bids. I liked the unusual, high watermark, non-cumulative scoring/payment mechanism. I would play this one again, but would not suggest it.

RATING: 7
The only real complaint I have with this game is the fact that is it can go too long for what it is.  Two of the regularly occurring phases involve somewhat open-ended player interaction.  There is an auction phase and a trading phase.  If folks plan ahead, know what they want, and what they are willing to give up, the game can move along nicely.  I really love this game.  I think it is absolutely beautiful.  There is a nice balancing mechanism that rewards the leading player with a bonus tile each turn but requires him to give up an asset token to the player in last place.  I've read that some gamers were a little disappointed that the components were not three dimensional.  Sure, that might have been nice.  However, I really like the look of the game as it is.  I believe that this game's reputation has suffered from the fact that it had the unenviable distinction of being the next Days of Wonder game after Battle Lore.  It isn't Battle Lore.  And it isn't Cleopatra and the Society of Architects.  But, it is a beautiful, interesting, fun, well-balanced game that I highly recommend.

[b]Colosseum[/b] was sold via the BGG marketplace. 

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]As much as I enjoyed this game my family did not. My original thoughts still hold true, but I ended up trading it away because it was not going to get played. The one thing everyone enjoyed was the trading/wheeling and dealing and once we got [thing=20437][/thing] I unfortunately knew it was all over for Colosseum. This just didn't click with my family and since we try to keep our collection lean it left.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (9):[/COLOR]

[i]- Easy to teach to my family on our weekly game night.
- The theme is awesome and well integrated into the game.
- A great introduction to auctions and trading for a family game.
(We do not trade one at a time, but instead everyone trades at once).
- There is a helpful catch up mechanic for the person in last place that only affects the person in first (which actually adds some extra strategy into the game.
- The scoring: you only score your best round (out of 5).
- The artwork and production quality is top notch.[/i]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

*中央市場擺滿背面是綠色的表演板塊，之後3/4/5人每人發8/6/5塊綠色的表演板塊，3/4人時剩下一個蓋著放一旁，橘色背的全部丟袋中，對應米寶放在圖板對應位置，11~30節目企劃照同樣長度疊起放一旁，遊戲回合標記放在對應位置
*每個玩家拿一個計分標記放0分跟一個表演場，30元，根據不同玩家人數把表演場放在對應的位置，將1~5節目企劃隨激發每人一張，再發6~10每人一張，剩下的移除，表演場在帝王米寶左邊的拿起始標記
*遊戲進行五輪，每輪有五個階段，每個階段行動都可選擇是否執行，每個階段都由起始開始行動，輪完之後進入下個階段，每輪結束時，回合標記下移，起始標記左傳，在第五輪的第四階段後結束
1.投資 每個人只能做一個投資行動，四選一
>支付節目企劃右上金額，拿一個比之前表演數字更大的節目企劃
>支付10元來買一個表演場擴展(可選怎往哪個方向擴張)，最多擴兩次
>支付10元，拿一個季票板塊放在你的表演場
>支付5而買一個帝王包廂放在你的表演場，只能買一次
2.獲得表演板塊  每人可以最多競標買到一個市場板塊一次，起始開始選一區開始標，底價都是8元，標到的拿走並退出此階段，不是指定競標玩家標到不補充，繼續指定下個競標市場，如果是指定競標市場玩家標到則從袋中抽表演板塊(先把每用到的綠背用完)補充所有市場，然後由該玩家的下家開始指定競標(指定競標玩家也可單純跳過此時也會補充，跳過指定的玩家之後沒機會在指定)
3.交易表演板塊  從起始開始輪流每人有機會拿出表演板塊出來交易，此時錢跟表演板塊都是交易籌碼，不能用未來承諾當作籌碼，所有人都交易後結束，只能跟當時的主動玩家交易
4.表演  米寶會帶來額外觀眾，帝王/執政官/議員/分別帶7/5/3個
>玩家輪流擲骰(有帝王包廂的丟兩顆)，每顆可以移動米寶最多同骰點的格數，順時鐘移動，如果米寶停在休息格，拿帝王獎牌(兩顆移動同個時只看最後位置)
>出示1張要表演的計畫，表演場大小要超過需求，然後放想使用的表演板塊，可使用上回合的(如果使用新的計畫，之前表演過的會翻面額外加5觀眾)，根據支付的表演板塊缺額拿對應分數，每個之前的表演五觀眾，每張季票加5觀眾，每個明星加4觀眾，然後根據米寶額外加觀眾，紀念獎台3觀眾，可支付帝王獎章加3觀眾，移動分數標記到對應觀眾數格，如果分數比之前低就不動，拿對應此回合觀眾數金錢
5.目前分數最高的拿一座紀念獎台，平手比錢多，在平手丟骰，之後丟掉一個使用的表演板塊，然後分數最低的跟此回合拿紀念獎台的玩家指定拿一個一般的表演板塊，平手比錢少再丟骰(最後輪沒有此階段)
*遊戲第五輪結束時分數最高的玩家獲勝，平手比錢，在比明星數
***特殊表演板塊***
>萬用卡  可替代任意但不能獲得巨星獎勵
>皇室獎章  拿到就丟掉換一個帝王獎牌
>額外行動  可再投資階段棄掉做兩次投資或是換帝王獎牌
***巨星獎勵***
第一個收集到某種表演板塊三個的拿走，之後有人更多就拿走，如果該玩家少於三個且有人單獨有三個要給他，多人平手就放回
***帝王獎牌***  利用貴族拿時只能藉由擲骰移動時拿取
>丟一個可以表演時加3分
>丟一個移動貴族時多動1~3格，可順或逆時鐘，一定要再擲骰移動後做
>丟一個拿六元
>丟兩個時可再投資階段再做一次額外投資
****變體競標規則***
招募演員階段時，已成功競標取得演員道具卡的玩家，若非為起標玩家，仍無法參與原起標玩家繼續發起的競標行動。但之後其他玩家擔任起標玩家時，則可繼續參與競標
***2人變體***
*起始一樣每人發八個表演板塊，市場只填滿四個
*競標階段結束後，兩個人各選一個市場區清空補新的
*起始創造一個六個表演板塊的市場，交易階段每個人可以2換1來換一次，之後全部蓋著洗勻棄掉四個，在抽兩個補，棄掉的除了特殊版塊都丟回袋中
*舞台一個人用兩個，擴建時支付一次一起擴建，兩個不相連，每回合表演時要兩個輪流
*跟市場交易次數不限制
**2人玩4人變體**
*兩個舞台所有東西都分開
*競標時，每人都主動玩家兩次，選區時另外玩家要支付起標價買走或是跳過就結束由主動玩家買走，被買走時下次就直接挑一區付8元買，拿到表演物件時要自行分配到兩個表演區中
*交易只能跟對手的舞台交易
*落後玩家跟第一玩家拿到表演物件時可任選一個舞台放
*遊戲最後兩個較低分的為你的分數

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The components are wonderful. The downside of the biggest score feature is that your result can hinge on the non-appearance of one lousy tile or one that won't get traded to you because you didn't have anything attractive enough in return (or the others have their tiles already). In a 3 player game, with more tiles, the penalty of losing tiles by being in front is somewhat diminished. The ability to move 2 nobles seems overly powerful (earning both medals and points) and therefore the investment decisions aren't as interesting as they might have been. With more players, the pain of someone else targetting the same work as you is overly impactful. All in all, too unbalanced and arbitrary to get a higher rating, but still worthy of an occasional play.

RATING: 8.5
This is a great game and I consider it to be one of the most dashingly beautiful games in my collection. This is production value at its absolute best! Unfortunately this game has a little problem: The last round is often a "victory give away". If several players are running for victory, the winner will be determined by who has the pieces to allow the victory! It's sad but true. I still love the game, but I'd give it an even better rating if it wasn't for this faulty fact! The game is still awesome, amazingly interactive, competitive on every level and I love to bedazzle newcomers to the hobby with this one!

- Rules are top-notch, very nicely illustrated, with important examples and well organized. Easy to learn and easy to explain, but players might need one or two rounds to get started!

RATING: 7
I purchased this game on a whim for our gamer group. I felt the theme and game mechanics were similar to other games played in the past.

We ended up playing it twice. The second game was drastically different from the first. Once everyone got a feel for the game and understood the mechanics, the second game got tighter. Trading and acquirement of assets became cutthroat. 

I wouldn't suggest this game as an introduction game to non gamers. As for us, this game will have definite repeat-ability.

12/28/11 - After a year of owning the game, it doesn't get requested for play as much as I would like. I think it is a fine game but I am starting to see a certain trend appear in the game play. Anyone who takes the time to purchase a loge, is more likely to win then those that don't buy it. 
I am not saying this is a guaranteed path to victory, but it is powerful. Using two dice to move two or one pawns into your Colosseum or onto colored squares to draw the medals is just a solid strategy.

The major complaint I get is usually about the bidding stage and trade phase. I have never tried the variants since I usually play it with new players. I would say most people rank it a 6 on the BGG rating.

Goal Met :star::star::star::star::star: $1 or less per play

I have heard a lot about this game on the geek but it is OOP and I couldn't fork out $100 for it until one lucky day when this game fell literally on my lap from a board game giveaway. This game didn't disappoint me after the first play. Out of all the mechanisms in the game, I like the trading aspect the best. I would love to play again. ---- Update ----
Sold it while it is hot. Don't know if Asmodee will reprint it with DoW under them so might as well clear some shelf space.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
In this game you control a Colosseum, and try to put on the best event possible across all of the players across all five rounds of the game. Effectively, your score is your best turn. Turns build on each other as you play, and the core piece is collecting the resources you need through bidding and trading. One element that's funny to think about is that you are allowed put on shows with less tiles than the event needs for fewer points. For example, a boat show with no boats, or a lion show with no cages. The magic of this game is also it's flaw. The bids could not go your way, or the players driving the trades might not trade evenly, or with you at all. It's not a bad game, but the idea that a win or loss might not be something you can effect with your own choices and actions keeps this from being a great game.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
My somewhat uncharitable review of this one goes like this:

Do you enjoy the game-long strategic planning of an upgrade path?
-> Play Princes of Florence

Do you enjoy the set-collection via auctions of mixed lots?
-> Play Hollywood Blockbuster

Do you enjoy the trading of tiles for mutual benefit?
-> Play Chinatown

Do you enjoy the gamey manipulation of position to gain from the catch-up mechanism?
-> Play Power Grid

Do you enjoy the roll-and-move of the Emperor and Senators?
-> Play Snakes & Ladders

RATING: 7
this game ought to be a comfortable 8, maybe even an 8.5, but after three plays, I've rated it a 7.  Each game ran awfully long, at around 2.5 hours every time.  Now part of that might be down to th erules explaination necessary each time, though I think that the 60-90 minutes on the box is hopelessly optimistic.

There's lots going on (and although it reminded me a bit of Leonardo, I much prefer Colosseum); you have to prioritise your wants, build up your infrastructure, and plan carefully for the last two turns long before you get there.  Unlike Leonardo, I liked Colloseum a lot.  

I disagree with criticism that I've seen regarding the movement of the nobles - some games they whizz around the board, sometimes they are more static, but it is planning - rather than luck - that should determine your last turn's score.  
Whether there are enough important decisions to sustain most of us for over two hours is another matter.  The game runs such a long time because there is an asset auction phase and an asset trading phase every turn - I can't help but think that with some adjustment to either mechanism, one or other of these two phases could have been done away with entirely, shaving half an hour off the playing time. 

The problem is that there are two separate (and legthy) ways to distribute asset tokens to players - an auction round, then a trading round.  I cannot believe that there wasn't some better mechanism to allow people to get what they want but pay for the privelege (an Alhambra-style draft, for example), which would have taken far less time than the current combination.

RATING: 6
It just seemed to me, that Colosseum uses many mechanics (bidding/auction, set collecting, trading, and choice of show tickets) but none of them are implemented very well. Compared to other games that use them, the gameplay for each mechanic is weak. This could all add up to a fun time, but as a game Colosseum seemed not up to par with many other games. It is too fiddly and long for what it is, and has some playtesting flaws (leader could/should sandbag instead of getting stolen from, and the timing of loss of Star Performers is flawed). Recommended only if you're looking for another game to learn and have 3 hours that wouldn't be better spent playing other games you know are very good.

RATING: 6
My favorite aspect of Colosseum is the theme.  It's fun to build a Roman Gladiator show.  I also enjoy the player interaction.  There is player interaction in every phase. Some of it is in the "screw your opponent style" while some of it is in the "share with my opponents to my advantage."   I enjoy the variety of types of interaction.  I also like the way scoring works because not every round has to be a winning round.  You can have a money producing round.

There is a lot of the game that is based on the tiles you draw at the beginning.  I could see it being difficult to play if you got two shows that were entirely mismatched with your initial tile draws.  No one had that problem in the only game I've played, but I could see it being an issue.

Additionally, there is something weird with how the tiles are traded between players and how they are acquired.  It makes Colosseum less fun than I wanted it to be.

The components are really fun!  The detailed Senators, Consuls, and Emperor are neat and add a lot to the gameplay.

RATING: 7
Components are well done and the theme is well developed. It is even fun to play, to some extent. My initial complaint against the game was that the gap between first and last player would always be great. It seemed first player was condemned to finish last during the last round because of all the pieces he would lose. When we instituted a new rule that last player could steal from either first or the game board, we experienced a change in the game. The last became a challenger for last round and the first did not necessarily lose all of his/her tiles.
Therefore, enjoyability has been increased and I would not increase my rating from 6.8 to 7.0.

RATING: 9.5
I was _very_ leary of purchasing this one since I generally find Wolfgang Kramer's games to have interesting well-balanced mechanics that never quite fit with the theme.

This game is an exception to that generalization, and is by far the most enjoyable of all Kramer's games I have played to date.

Yes, the game is a set collection game but it doesn't _feel_ like a set collection game.

The mechanics are well thought out and gameplay moves quickly. 

Perhaps one of the best endorsements is that both times we have played it everyone was left wanting 'just one more turn' because throughout the game it has a feel that you're working toward your goal of putting your show together.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.2
This game has high quality components and a fantastic theme. Who wouldn't like assembling gladiators and beasts and chariots to put on a show in ancient Rome before not only the Senators and Consul but the Emperor himself?

However, two of the mechanisms include trading and auctions, some of my least favorites. Also, you have a lot of things to remember when you score. You have to remember that you get extra points for season tickets, for having a podium, for having one or more star performers, for having the Emperor in the crown and for having medals and more.

A little too much bookkeeping for the players but an overall enjoyable experience.

RATING: 5
After a disapointing first play, I come to the conclusion that while this is a fun game to play...  I find it's balance to be lacking.  It seems to me that there is definately one, maybe two ways to go about winning this game.  I enjoyed building up my retinue, and trying to manage a production each turn.  In my opinion the Emporer's medals are so lucky that it hurts game play.  Versatility and power of that kind should be managed much better.  In our game, the Emporer medal mix amoung the four players was:  Winner 7, 2nd place 5, 3rd place 2, and last place 0.  In the last turn the winner was able to cash in 4 or 5 medals for an additional 12-15 points...  which alone beat my score for the first round.   I would play this again, but don't quite look forward to it since I feel that the winning strategy is scripted.
We also played the more than one auction win possible variant which made Emporer medals and your ability to cash them in for $6 each even more versatile.  

in the end I feel that this is the type of game that tries too hard to add randomness and does it in the wrong way...  event / special cards would have been better than the rol and move pieces.

RATING: 7
J'ai entendu dire beaucoup de mal de Colosseum dans certains milieux ludiques... Le jeu ne le mérite sans doute pas. Sans avoir évidemment la richesse stratégique d'un [gameid=555], la phase d'échanges rend le jeu très interactif et beaucoup plus fun. Le thème est très bien rendu et le matos tout à fait réussi (même si mon beau-frère a repéré des erreurs de grammaire sur certains textes latins…) Dans la catégorie des intermédiaires entre les jeux familiaux et les « gros jeux », il fonctionne très bien…

L'équilibre et la tension sont bonnes, les enchères fonctionnent bien, avec une petite part de hasard qui vient faire la différence entre un jeu « pour joueurs » et un jeu « familial ». Si les échanges ne trainent pas trop, le jeu garde une bonne fluidité… Par contre, dans le cas contraire, il faut bien admettre que ça peut devenir bien longuet.

RATING: 7
Ranking could improve more with more plays and experimenting with auctions. I enjoy the thin theme. I feel that money should hold a points value at the end to the game. The advanced auctions (intense auctions), opens more strategic options, such as trying to maximise your income, potentially having a token stolen, but using your wealth to win more auctions. I enjoy playing without the trading phase to speed the game up considerably. Other advantages to ditching the trading is that it offsets the higher number of tiles you win using the intense auction variant, speeds the game up, and stops what can sometimes be trade silliness between two players helping each other out just a little too much. Nice bits and pretty board.

After further play the only beef I have with this game is the trading phase has the potential to ruin it. It becomes too easy to put on maximum scoring games as players make big trades to offload all their junk. Anyone caught out without the right "junk" to trade is doomed. So what was the point of all the finely balanced and carefully deliberated auctions? Pity, because I really enjoy it without trading, but I'm not at liberty to just make executive rules decisions for the other players.

RATING: 7
Elso jatek utani tapasztalat: A jatek valami hihetetlen modon ki van egyensulyozva. Harman jatszottunk, az egyik jatekos a legnagyobb eloadasra gyujtott (tobb dolgot is hatrebb szoritva ezzel), a masik szezon jegyekkel, gyozelmi podiumokkal tomte teli a stadionjat (ez viszont a stadion bovites rovasara ment), en meg inkabb hagytam elmenni a negyedik kort (nem inditottam egy ujabb, nagyobb jatekot, hanem egy korabbit jatszottam ujra). A vegeredmeny? Kettonknek 84 pontja lett (1 arany dontott az elsosegert) es az a jatekos aki elvileg egy kisebb rendezvenyt hajtott vegre a stadionjanak a korlatozottsaga miatt, csak 5 ponttal maradt le (79 pont). A jatek eleg gyorsan lement annak ellenere, hogy mindannyian a sotetben tapogatoztunk egy idegig. Az ujrajatszasi faktorat magasra teszem, es megmerem kockaztatni, hogy a tovabbi jatekokban akar feljebb is mehet a pontszam. Tarsaim szerint nem annyira csaladi jatek, ellentetben az en velemenyemmel, ezert majd inditok egy tesztet otthon. Ja, es gyonyoruuuuuuu a jatek!
Update1: A jateknak nem kell hazi szabaly, 5-en nagyon jo volt, meg szeretnek vele jatszani
Update2: Ismet 5 szemelyes parti, paran jo sokat gondolkoztunk. Aki nem szereti az olyan jatekokat ahol keves az interakcio es indonkent jo par percet varni kell a sajat korere, azoknak nem ajanlott.
Update3: Kezd egyre jobban tetszeni.

RATING: 5
First: Great and overproduced bits. And there are some mechanisms and ideas that I really like. But there are others that seem genuinely broken. For instance, the Emperor is worth the most points of all the nobles. But in a 4-player game, it would be nearly impossible for him to make his way around the track to the 4th player. How is that not broken?! Also seems it would be prone to kingmaking, since everything is trackable. And what's the deal with banging on the "leader" by letting those in the rear take a free tile? He's not really the leader, since it's the final score at the end that counts.  

Would I play again? Maybe. Would I suggest it? Not likely.

RATING: 8
[4 plays] My first learning game was very interesting, and it was obvious to me that the long term goal of best production wasn't necessarily a linear progression of 5 productions.

In my second game, against the lesson learned in my first game, I continued to play from the lead. It came down to a very tight finish. Perhaps playing suckhole until the end is truly an equally valid path to victory. Not sure how I feel about that in terms of great game design. It seems like at least in Power Grid you can't lag so terribly far behind and still remain in strong contention.

Anyway, I do think this is a well produced game, has interesting decisions, a negotiation phase that can be dramatic, and more depth than is apparent at first. Barb very much enjoyed the game, and that always pleases me.

Most recent play Aug 2013 with  Michael and Bob.

RATING: 5
[2011-04-30]

Jó játékötlet, gyönyörű megvalósítás, izgalom, hogy sikerül-e megtervezni a kinézett cirkuszi játékot. Sajnos csak ennyi pozitívumot tudok elmondani a játékról, pedig szerettem volna, ha megtetszik. Nézzük fázisonként a játékot.
Vásárlás: Arénánk bővítése, új játék beszerzése, vezetők pozíciójának nagyobb változtatása vagy VIP páholy vétele. Ez a rész teljesen rendben van, tényleg stratégiai döntés, hogy ezek közül melyik körben mire megy a játékos.
Licitálás hármanként véletlenszerűen feljövő cirkuszi elemekre. Na itt kezdődnek a gondok. Sajnos sokat jelent, hogy milyen hármasok jönnek ki, az pedig, hogy adott körben ki a kezdőjátékos még fontosabb. Pontosabban nem jó kezdőjátékosnak lenni és aki a játék végén lesz az, elég nagy hátrányt kell bekalkulálnia. Valahogy ezt a részt jobban át kellett volna gondolni.
Cserélgetés: Féltem ettől a résztől, nem is megalapozatlanul. Egyrészt ez a rész adott típusú játékosokkal nagyon meghosszabbíthatja a játékot, másrészt az elején még sokat cserélnek általában, a játék végén szinte semmi sem eladható, cserélhető. Nagyon sok fölös lapka bennragadhat, amivel igazából semmit nem lehet kezdeni. Nem eladható, nem beváltható. Ezen is dolgozhattak volna még.
Vezetők mozgatása, játék megrendezése: A legötletesebb rész a legnagyobb szerencsefaktor a játékban sajnos. A városi vezetők körben mozgása és császári kegy, illetve cirkuszi játékokon való részvételük egyrészt a kockadobás, másrészt a többi játékos miatt óriási szerencsefaktor a játékban, ami azonos képességű játékosok esetében simán dönt a játék végeredményéről.
Jó játék lehetett volna a Colosseum, de sajnos nem lett jó. Igazából az utolsó forduló dönt mindenről és az arra legszerencsésebben kijövő megszerzendő lapkák, illetve vezetők helyzete. Nagyon nagy a szerencsefaktor, állítom nagyobb, mint a Stone Age-ben, ami sajnos egy ilyen komolynak tűnő játéknál már bőven sok. Nem csak egy út van a győzelemhez, talán még az újrajátszhatósága sem rossz a játéknak, de sajnos úgy érzem az elsőségről a szerencse dönt.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Even with drafting, auctions, trading and a catch-up mechanism the game still doesn't feel balanced.  If an early leader breaks ahead they will continue to be first and will maintain the lead from both the bonus points gained from being in the lead along with the money earned.  With no friction in money there isn't much stopping the leader.  The die movement of the senators is just way too lucky for my taste.  The favor tiles are just way too powerful not to get them.  If you never roll the die/dice that allow for you to garner favors, you are way behind the curve.  The shopping aspect of this is kind of fun, and trying to meet the requirements can be satisfying, but most of the information essential to make worthwhile decisions isn't revealed so you are often just stabbing at the dark with your "strategy".  I'm going to build this mixture up in hopes that player x doesn't grab it first.  At the end of the game many of the upgrades are crap as people burned through tiles leaving too few to make the big games worthwhile.  This leaves a number of other useless tiles.  I also feel that there aren't enough joker tiles.  Because they are so powerful and because they are fairly rare, the price that one has to pay to get one can be awfully high or for eight, depending on when it was drawn.  

I'm hoping you can't legally trade to get the bonuses to break ties and trade back to the same players in the same trading round.  That almost breaks the bonuses, rather makes them really irritating.

There is just too much luck in this game for the length and required processing.

RATING: 2
Really, really enjoy this game.  Lots of player interaction between both the trading and the auctions.  Putting on the various shows allows you to plan a strategy, so you've also got a goal on what you're working on.  Dice-rolling to move the pieces (senators, emperor, etc.) around the board for bonus points.

UPDATE: While I do like this one, I have to downgrade it to an 8, as it's a bit long, sometimes the auctions offer little choice, and it's not quite as fluid or interesting as other games.  Nevertheless, it's still one that I enjoy getting to the table.

UPDATE #2: Ok, I've now played this a third time and this game, while quite pretty, has very little contention, nearly no player interactions, and few meaningful decisions despite its generally two-hour play time. I hope this never sees the table again.

RATING: 8
This was a game that looked interesting for quite some time and I had heard a lot of good things about but was never easily available until this year, 2017 when I acquired the reprint from TMG.  This is an auction game where players compete to put on the most popular Colosseum show.

The game is actually reasonably simple as far as rules set.  Each round players will invest in one item (with some exceptions), bid on assets needed for shows, put on shows earning them points and money, and do this across 5 rounds.  Instead of accumulating points the winner at the end is the player who earned the most points during a single round via the show they put on.

Reading the rules, I was initially afraid that there wouldn't be enough surprise factor when it came to the winning point total.  I thought everyone would have their largest show on the final turn and it would be pretty easy to tell at a glance who was the winner.  In my games so far that has not been the case.  I've seen players finish with their largest score not being the final round as well as been surprised at who won at the end.  That was a nice surprise.  Something else I didn't initially grasp was the importance of the investment phase.  I initially thought the most important phase would be the auction but there are so many things needed and you can only acquire one each investment phase that sometimes tough choices need to be made.  Money can also be extremely tight.

I've also found that the game changes drastically based on player count and that there is an awful lot of maneuvering possible when it comes to the auciton phase which offers a lot of choice depth which I really liked.

Aside from the metal coins I'm not sure how pleased I am with the reprint comparing it to the awesome Days of Wonder version.  My biggest criticism/fear is that the game is somewhat solvable.  There doesn't appear to be enough variance and with only 5 turns each game I'm worried that a veteran player would fall into the same strategies making future games dull.  For now though I quite enjoy it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
That Colosseum works as well as it does is because of its central novelty: "high water mark" scoring. Each player has five opportunities to stage a show, and their final score is their best result. Players are both rewarded (tiles that improve the value of future shows) and punished (the lowest-scoring player can steal from the highest) for doing well, so an early lead is not necessarily a positive.

The game's other mechanisms are each so shaky that the not-unplayable combination is almost miraculous. Of players' five guaranteed opportunities to purchase upgrades, at least four are required to be competitive and all that's left is some quibbling over ordering. I haven't tried the "intense auctions" variant but the basic auction is both utterly lacking in tension -- the lack of scarcity means that most lots will go for the base amount -- and a significant source of luck for players later in turn order. The large number of different token types mean that most trades will be two players trading their trash for another's, but also drags out the game if committed traders attempt to wheedle.

And then there's the roll and move: roaming consuls, senators and the emperor are moved each round, granting medals if they stop on specific spaces or bonus points if in a player's colosseum at showtime. Some manipulation is possible but it is just an unnecessary source of swing.

The components are lovely but with the basic rules it's not worth playing at all, let alone at the excessive length it's likely to clock in at. Wouldn't mind giving it another go with an auction variant.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5

The number of turns seems a bit short by most euro standards, but it works okay.  I only got to buy two events to put on beyond my two starting ones in 5 turns.  Had to use a fair number of actions expanding my coliseum.

RATING: 5.5
Boy I WANT to really like a game this pretty.  But Colosseum seems to go on for -far- too long for what it is.  And, honestly, I don't like that you have to decide what you're doing for the entire game after the first or second round.  The game's a bit better if the you make the "trading turns" into a free-for-all timed trading period.  But it just doesn't click well with me.  Plus, I really don't like games where your final score is based entirely on a single play (see Power Grid).  I like feeling like I'm accumulating and building to something.  While it's true your last performance is usually your best, I don't like knowing that I might really hit my best game on the third or fourth turn, and the rest of the game will end up being pointless for me if so.  Maybe there's some hidden magic here I'm missing, but despite how beautiful the game is, it just doesn't do anything for me.

RATING: 5
I like a lot about this game, but there are one or two 'shows' that can get blocked easily and it is kinda game breaking for me.

This is very nearly a great game, but doesn't quite work for me.

Update: Okay, there's actually a lot here I don't like. Loges seem overpowered to the point whereby not having one makes the game a little pointless. Why not all just start with one and roll two dice? Why give the illusion of variable strategies if the others don't really work?

Ideally, I'd actually remove the dice and the pawns altogether and probably tweak the way money is generated. I'd rather have more money in the beginning so that bidding is more interesting and less at the end so that everyone doesn't just throw all their money at the final auction. I also dislike the way the tiles are so variable that one random draw of tiles can be worth +/- 50 money to a player. It's also a little disappointing when, with half the game to go, players can see they have no chance of winning. I'm sure that with repeat plays/better play that wouldn't happen but it always feels as much like poor design as poor play when it does happen, especially in such a family orientated/DoW game.

Rating down from 6.8 to 5.

RATING: 2
Initial Impression--Hollywood Blockbuster with way too many added game elements: throw in essentially unlimited trading, roll-and-move, over-complex special action rules, construction, insuperable stop-the-leader mechanics--and you get a kitchen-sink game that plays long and is too busy for fun.

Second Impression--Advanced rules kill the power of the stop-the-leader mechanic. Still, if your first two shows have no commonality, you're screwed. Likewise if you're collecting the same as another player and he gets to the show you want first. The game has essentially no mechanisms for recovering from having tiles incompatible with a show you're stuck with, and weak mechanisms for risk management. The coupling of chariots with horses and ships with gladiators make these pairs far harder to collect, signal your intentions obviously, and have no balancing value--and thus, chariots and ships are worthless for trading: add very, very badly balanced to Coloseum's list of faults. I expect my rating to drop to a 2 after one or two more plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I had the pleasure of trying this out last night.  While at first observation the game looks like it might have a learning curve it really doesn't.  You can basically learn the mechanics within the first round, but thats not to say that it doesn't have hidden complexity.  Its dripping in theme, with the right players you can feel like you are a grand director of events in glorious ancient rome. 

Bribery and subtle underhanded mechanics shine through while you try to put forthought into your next performance. Your constantly kept on your toes by the pawns moving around and the favor system.  You must continually think ahead about the events you desire to run and set personal goals if you wish to lead.  All lessons you learn early on.  Another great mechanic is the majority special performers, it made denying your opponents of them fantastic, especially when you don't necessarily need the roles for your events. I loved the rolling of the big pawns, theres interesting thought and dynamic in the Ceasar's favor tiles.  At first glance I thought it would be a lame pawns moving around the board mechanic, turns out theres plenty of planning and strategy with it.  All and all the gameplay is quite enjoyable and I loved the player interaction in this title and look very much forward to playing it again.

RATING: 6
Tough to puzzle out exactly why, but Colosseum din't impress as much as I'd hoped. I love auctions, the theme  of putting on spectacles is cool and the bits are great. Days of Wonder puts on a spectacle of its own with its overall production values. Nevertheless, Colosseum fell a bit flat in my initial plays. The game dragged and seemed a bit unpolished. I'll be playing this again, but at the moment I'm certainly unconvinced this is a game with any kind of staying power.

RATING: 5
Wolfgang Kramer might just be my favorite designer, but this one left me feeling flat.  The production values were top notch for this Days of Wonder release, but I can't see myself ever wanting to play this instead of Princes of Florence, unless I'd somehow managed to play Princes of Florence so many times that I'd eventually tired of it.  I suppose it's okay for a designer to copy so extensively from one of his own previous designs, but the similarities to Princes were really remarkable.  This of course means that Colosseum isn't a terrible game, given that a mimics a great game so closely, but as a result it's not a worthwhile game, in my view.  It doesn't really add anything, and just takes away things.  The idea of only counting your best show for your score is intriguing, but not something that worked well in practice, in contrast to counting all your shows or works in Princes.  The components were nice, but that's about all I can say on this game's behalf.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
When Colosseum came out, it was not exaggerated to say that it was the most underrated DOW game to come out from the big box series. In fact, the game was so underwhelming that Colosseum was part of a "buy 2 get 1" free promo for Christmas where it was "given" away for free if someone purchased another DOW big box game a full price.

Fast forward 10 years, the game is now in such a high demand that it routinely reaches triple digits for sales in BGG Marketplace. Scores and legions of fans claim that this is one of the most underrated gem and hard to find OOP in the DOW stable of games. So, is this game all hype or does it have a place on your shelf?

One of my geekbuddies mentioned that Colosseum plays similar to Princes of Florence. I did not even notice the comparison until it was pointed out. True, there are many similarities between both games, but there are also many distinct differences. But in my mind, this game is inferior to POF in many aspects. But first, let me do the pros. Colosseum is gorgeously produced not unlike other DOW games. That is a given by now. The pieces are solid the the components top notch. The tiles are also beautifully designed. While there are a lot of fiddly bits, the game is quite simple and repetitive in nature. It is very teachable and the rules are easy to digest. Non-gamers appear to embrace the game quite rapidly as well. So this game succeeds in drawing in the casual crowd much more readily than POF.

Now, the minuses: First, Colosseum has a huge luck component not present in POF. That remains my biggest gripe for the game. Moving the nobles around to add more spectators is crucial for the game and it is not wrong to say that many other variables do not contribute as big a swing as nobles do. Sure, the program values and investments also contribute a large part to the spectator count, but these values are more static and can be anticipated to a certain degree. Not so nobles with dice rolls. This dice rolling phase is also an area rife with kingmaking decisions. Even if you don't score, you can push other nobles toward or away from other players. The second gripe comes from the auction mechanism. The original rules have auction phases that are too loose and resulted in non-competitive auctions. Variants have been posted but the original rules should not have made the auction so toothless. There were just too many  choices and players just passed all the time, resulting in auction lots going between 8-10 gold each run. Finally, I found the single path victory to be rather limiting. Only five rounds are played and the options for investing are limited. I felt pressured to expand the arena and purchase programs. These actions will constitute 4/5 of investment options. While you can argue this is part of the game, I felt limited most of the time with my path selection. A large production is likely needed if you want to win the game, so expansion of arena and programs purchases feel like a must to compete. 

So is Colosseum worthwhile? In my mind, if you have POF, then you can pass on Colosseum. It is not nearly as good as POF. However, given DOW's production qualities and general love for all things Kramer, I think the game will see multiple copies sold upon the reprint by TMG, followed slowly by sinking back to oblivion once that craving is satisfied by the vocal few.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
A fantastic game with an awesome theme, and enough bling and quality Games of Wonder cardboard to make it a pleasure to play.  Only three things stop me rating Colosseum a 10: 
Firstly, the inter-player asset trading can be unstructured and dithery.  Without threats of slow players being fed to lions, this phase can drag. Still, if you liked it in Catan, you'll love it in this. Me? Not so much. 
Secondly, the game seems to finish just as your engine is getting lift. One more turn? Hard to say. Given the nature of the scoring mechanism this might penalize truly efficient players by allowing catch up but, my game group house-rules this. 
Lastly, it suffers from a 'rich get richer' problem that is exacerbated by the podium awards to the winner of each round. Still, in the world of colosseum ownership you're only as good as your best performance and, a come-from-behind victory is not out of the question!

RATING: 5.5
Played once, five player. 

I didn't really care for this one much. There's nothing about it I find terribly offensive -- it just didn't pack enough fun into the two-plus hours we spent playing it. I'll give it another go but I have a couple nagging concerns I just don't see going away:

- 'Optimal' building strategy seems to be dictated by the turn order (not a critical flaw but I would to have at least a couple viable options). And with only 5 rounds, anything less than optimal isn't going to win.

- The potential for king-making seemed high (one ill-advised trade can (and did) cause a major shift among the majority bonuses). 

That said, Colosseum is not without merit. It is attractive, it has a considerable amount of player interaction (one area where it improves upon Princes of Florence which, although I enjoy, is very solitaire-ish), and it was thematically strong -- it felt like you were building towards a climatic showdown of gala events.

RATING: 4
Takes the basic model of Princes of Florence, unnecessarily complicates some parts of the game (set collecting), dumbs down others so that they are less interesting (the auctions, building), and then introduces a strong random element (the pawns) that seems to undermine the strategic aspects of the game (and don't even get me started on the trading, which annoyed me to no end). I don't mind games where you try to set yourself up as best as possible and then see where things fall, but not when they last 3 hours, require this much uninteresting mental effort, and then you see the winner decided by a die roll on the last turn. Acquiesced to playing this a couple times cause it's a favorite in one of my groups, but I'd be hard pressed to sit down to this again for a long time.

RATING: N/A
In Colosseum each player is a Roman impresario - producing great spectacles in his or her arena in the hopes of attracting the most spectators. Players earn wealth and glory for each event run, using it to create ever more ambitious events. They will need to improve their arena, find the best performers, lure the Emperor and his nobles, and manage assets for long-term success to be granted the title of Grand Impresario. Colosseum includes: a large game board representing arenas in cities across the Roman Empire where events are produced; 5 arenas; 10 arena expansions; 6 unique painted resin pawns representing the Emperor, Consuls and Senators; 5 Emperor's loges; 10 Season Tickets; 2 Roman dice; 90 Roman coins; 30 Event programs; 152 Event asset tokens; 7 Star Performer Awards; 18 Emperor Medals; 6 Event Summary sheets; 1 Storage bag; and a detailed Rules booklet.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a quirky game that seems to offer a lot of choices over the duration of the game.  It's unique also in that you're working toward a "best of" score, instead of a cumulative score in the game.  It's a little weird, and takes a little getting used to as far as how to plan the game, but it provides a different experience from most games.  It seems like it's equally likely for a player to be competitive by using the same attraction over and over again, as it is to go for newer attractions each turn.  Although, it seems like the best strategy is to re-use a smaller attraction while working toward a larger one that's slightly related.  It's intriguing enough for me to want to play it again, with a better idea of how it works.

1 play: 5 players

RATING: 6
I had tons of fun the first time I played through this, but repeated plays reveal some serious problems. The worst offender is the catch-up mechanic that lets the player who is behind steal a tile. This rewards players for deliberately putting on awful shows, which is just terrible flavor. Another huge flaw is that money doesn't matter at all, almost, due to a decidedly broken auction system (most of the time auctions will go for the minimum bid).

On the other hand, putting on a massive full show has a certain appeal, and the basic ideas behind the game are solid. I just wish some more work had been put into fixing the broken mechanics.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Colosseum is a far more family friendly game than it appears at first glance. I remember receiving the game in a BGG trade and glancing quickly through the rules, my impression being that it seemed fairly complex. That feeling was reinforced after sorting through the huge supply of tokens and other cardboard bits, as I took inventory to make sure I wasn't missing anything. As it turns out, I was completely wrong. The game plays so smoothly my 9 year-old not only understood it, he kicked my tail. We even played with the official "gamer" auction variant posted in the Days of Wonder FAQ and the kids had no problem with either the game's rules or its strategies. I'm glad I took a chance on it, because auction games are typically a very hard sell to kids and non-gamers but I've found that Colosseum's awesome theme, colorful components and straight forward game play completely work for the casual crowd.

02-14-12 UPDATE: I recently traded this away after playing it a few more times and then getting a chance to try Princes of Florence. The two games certainly share a common lineage, but Princes of Florence has a ton more decision points and more importantly, more paths to victory. I still think that Colosseum is a pretty good game and that its theme and presentation are both absolutely killer, but my plays of Princes of Florence have been game changers. I enjoyed them immensely and do not foresee ever wanting to go back to the Colosseum, although I will probably miss those bad-ass Senator/Caesar pawns...

RATING: N/A
Going by the title I had always assumed this was a gladiators in the arena game. Wrong! Who knew that the colosseums were used for all kinds of varied shows from horse stunts, historical battle re-enactments, poetry, and of course gladiators versus lions. As impresarios, we are competing with the other arenas to get the attention of both the mob and the senators by putting on the grandest shows. I would say this game is a combination of Lords of Waterdeep for the set collections of arena assets and Drum Roll for the theme, attracting the best crowds and upgrading your venue. Lots of auction and barter--But this game pre-dates both comparisons. Throw in the classic roll and move to add a vintage mechanic for nostalgia.

RATING: 8
Wow. More fun than I would have thought. For a family game, I'm a bit surprised that the luck isn't softened (like it is in Princes of Florence). Nice art and production quality. Nice decisions. But the short 5-turn limit makes everything feel tense. I think the player with the Colosseum directly behind the Emperor is in a tough spot, especially in a 3 or 4 player game, as it's entirely possible (perhaps likely?) that the Emperor will never get that far. And being in the lead is a tough spot - you want to coast and draft, being in 2nd place as often as possible before a big final surge. So if you go first on the first turn AND you're behind the Emperor, you're really in for a tough game. Trading slows things down considerably, almost taking as much time as the rest of the game combined - which is a real drag if you happen to be on the outside looking in at people trading for mutually beneficial items and you're unable to participate. Interesting that only your single best event "counts" for your score, which several players found incredibly confusing. I'm actually surprised at how much I enjoy this.

RATING: 6.5
Admittedly I was pretty intimidated by the sheer number of pieces that came with the game.  Upon closer inspection, I really appreciated how well they were made, the production value for Colosseum was pretty high, with quality items and an awesome board.  The gameplay, on the other hand, I enjoyed but didn't love it.  I found the whole experience to be at least one round too short.  The auction mechanism felt a bit flawed (we played with the advanced rule in which everyone can bid on as many lots as they have the money to), allowing too much actor pieces to be available, and thus affected the trading.

Replayability is also another concern.  Since the number of plays you can perform are limited, I imagine after several sessions one would find himself repeating the same repertoire, and that, I'm afraid, can get stale.  So the judge is still out on this one for now.

Update: As feared Colosseum does become repetitive after a couple of plays.  Strategy is always the same, save up enough actors and the emperor's token for one huge presentation at the end.  I wouldn't be adversed to playing this, but I do think my time would be better spent on other games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.3
There are a lot of really interesting things going on here.  The game length, however, suffers significantly for each new player involved, and also suffers a large hit during the trading.  I think both problems could be solved by employing timers.  You may want to use a longer timer for the new players, out of fairness, but ultimately, playing through the whole game is what best gives a new player the skills to navigate the Colosseum machine next time.

The dignitaries are an interesting lot, as they provide players some very opportunistic options, but the players must be careful not to let them mound up in front of one player's arena, lest that player get, say, four to six of them to attend a single event near the end of the game.

When my first game (5p) finally ended, I was fairly relieved to move on to another game, but within a few hours, I was already looking forward to trying to bend that Colosseum machine to my will better next time!

All in all, this is a very interesting game, which feels familiar yet fresh; however, the whole affair takes a healthy chunk of time (easily 3-4 hours!).  It doesn't feel like it takes quite that long, but it does still feel a smidgen too long for what transpires.

Unfortunately, I think that the small amount of luck, since it is the spine of the game, makes or breaks your attempt at victory.  Playing excellently does not necessarily get you a victory over someone who played fairly well, but got lucky breaks.
